Ash'ari and Maturidi Solutions
Nasafi presents the Ash'ari and Maturidi responses to the objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari and Maturidi schools reconcile God's disapproval of disbelief with its actual occurrence by divine will.

Core Idea
Two schools offer distinct explanations: Ash'ari restricts the verse to believers; Maturidi explains approval as deeming good, which would negate punishment.

After the initial rebuttal, Nasafi presents two theological positions that directly answer the objection. The Ash'ari school interprets the Quranic verse as referring only to believers, while the Maturidi school distinguishes between divine pleasure (deeming good) and divine will (intending what occurs). Both preserve God's wisdom and justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the Ash'ari position
Read the part starting with 'ثم نقول: إن الأشعري يقول'. Notice how Ash'ari says God approves of disbelief as ugly and interprets the verse specifically.
This is a scriptural interpretation approach.
2
Read the Maturidi position
Read from 'وعلى قول مشايخنا' to the end. Focus on how Maturidis define approval (riḍā) as deeming good (istiḥsān) and why that cannot apply to disbelief.
This is a conceptual/linguistic approach.
3
Compare the two solutions
Notice how each school resolves the apparent contradiction. Ask: Which relies on textual specification? Which relies on semantic distinction?
Both preserve God's transcendence and justice.

Refuting the Mu'tazila on Divine Will and Oppression
Refutes Mu'tazila's argument using internal critique and linguistic analysis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi response to the Mu'tazila's use of the verse 'Allah does not intend oppression for the servants' relies on a critique of their concept of will and a linguistic analysis of the phrase.

Core Idea
The verse does not prove God cannot will others' oppression—only that He does not oppress.

The Mu'tazila often cite the Qur'anic verse 'And Allah does not intend oppression for the servants' to argue that God cannot will evil acts by creatures. In this passage, the author responds by first pointing out an internal inconsistency in the Mu'tazila's own doctrine of divine will, then providing a linguistic argument to show the verse's meaning is narrower. Let's examine this refutation step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazila argument
Recognize that the Mu'tazila use the verse to claim God cannot will oppression, and note the author's first move: he invokes the Baghdad school's own view that God is not described with real will, so the verse would mean something else on their terms.
The Baghdadis among the Mu'tazila deny that will is a real attribute of God.
2
Follow the linguistic analysis
The author cites linguists who explain that the phrase 'I do not intend your oppression' can have two meanings: either 'I do not intend that you be oppressed (by anyone)' or 'I do not intend to oppress you.'
This distinction is key to narrowing the verse's implication.
3
See how the author specifies the correct meaning
Based on prior evidences, the author argues that the intended meaning is that God does not oppress anyone Himself, not that He never wills oppression done by others.
Thus, the verse does not contradict the possibility of God willing acts of oppression by creatures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن تعلقهم بقوله تعالى ( وَمَا اللَّهُ يُرِيدُ ظُلْمًا لِلْعِبَادِ ) . قلنا على أصل البغدادين منكم لا يوصف الله تعالى بالإرادة حقيقة، وإذا ذكرت إرادته لفعله فمعناه أنه فعل فيكون تقدير الآية (٢) - على زعمهم – وما ظلم الله ظلمًا ونحن هكذا نقول فلم يكن لهم في الآية حجة. ثم نقول : إن أهل اللغة قالوا : إذا قال الرجل لآخر لا أريد ظلمك كان معناه لا أريد أن تظلم أنت من غير تعيين الفاعل، وإذا قال: لا أريد ظلمًا لك كان معناه لا أريد أن أظلمك. ونحن نقول : إن (۳) الله تعالى لا يريد أن يظلم أحدًا، على أن أكثر ما في الباب أن هذه اللفظة محتملة للمعنيين جميعًا إلا أنا نعين أحدهما وهو أن المراد منه لا أريد أن أظلمك لما سبق من الدلائل، والله الموفق.
And the answer to their attachment to His statement (and Allah does not intend oppression for the servants). We say: according to the principle of the Baghdadis among you, Allah Almighty is not described with will in reality, and if His will for His action is mentioned, its meaning is that He did it, so the estimation of the verse – according to their claim – is ‘And Allah did not oppress any oppression,’ and we say this, so there is no proof for them in the verse. Then we say: the linguists said: when a man says to another ‘I do not intend to oppress you,’ its meaning is ‘I do not intend that you be oppressed’ without specifying the doer, and when he says ‘I do not intend oppression for you,’ its meaning is ‘I do not intend to oppress you.’ And we say: Allah Almighty does not intend to oppress anyone, because the most that can be said is that this word is possible for both meanings, except that we specify one of them, which is that the intended meaning is ‘I do not intend to oppress you’ due to the preceding evidences, and Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 981

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila's argument based on the Qur'anic verse "And Allah does not intend oppression for the servants" (Ghafir 40:31). The author first responds by noting that according to the Baghdad school of the Mu'tazila, God is not described with will in a real sense; rather, when they speak of His will for an action, they mean that He performs it. Thus, the verse, on their own interpretation, would mean 'He did not commit oppression.' The author agrees with this, so the verse does not support their position. Next, the author presents a linguistic argument: the phrase 'I do not intend your oppression' can mean either 'I do not intend that you be oppressed (by someone else)' or 'I do not intend to oppress you.' The author asserts that the correct meaning here is that God does not intend to oppress anyone Himself, based on other evidences. Therefore, the verse does not negate that God may will acts of oppression done by others, as He is just in His decree.

Key Terms
إرادة (will)
ظلم (oppression)
بغداديون (Baghdadis)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse proves that God cannot will any evil acts, including those done by creatures.
Correction: The verse only negates that God Himself commits oppression, not that He wills oppression by others.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the key point of the linguists' analysis of the phrase 'I do not intend your oppression'?
A) It always means the speaker will not oppress anyone.
B) It can mean either not intending the listener to be oppressed or not intending to oppress the listener.
C) It only refers to preventing oppression by others.
D) It is ambiguous and cannot be interpreted.

Key Takeaway
The verse 'Allah does not intend oppression for the servants' does not prevent God from willing oppression caused by others; it only affirms His own justice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does this lesson's linguistic analysis challenge you to trust God's justice even when you don't understand His decree? What would it look like for you to submit to God's will without requiring Him to fit your standards of justice?
?
The author says the intended meaning is specified 'due to the preceding evidences.' Think of a time when you felt oppressed or witnessed injustice. What 'preceding evidences' in your faith or life can help you hold firmly to the belief that God Himself never oppresses, even when you cannot see the full picture? Are you willing to commit to that belief today, without demanding a full explanation?

The Mu'tazila Argument and the Ash'ari Response
Presents the Mu'tazila argument and the Ash'ari definition of foolishness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila argue that willing foolishness makes one foolish, and how the Ash'ari school refutes this by distinguishing between the witnessed and the absent, and by defining foolishness as that which is prohibited.

Core Idea
Foolishness is defined by prohibition; God is not prohibited, so He cannot be foolish.

The Mu'tazila present a rational argument: whoever wills foolishness is themselves foolish, based on human experience. But this analogy fails when applied to God, because the rules of the witnessed world do not automatically apply to the absent (God). In this lesson, we examine the initial objection and the Ash'ari response, which shifts the definition of foolishness to what is prohibited, thereby excluding God.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila argument
Read the first few lines where the Mu'tazila present their claim: 'The one who wills foolishness is foolish, by analogy to the witnessed.'
Notice the use of 'shahid' (witnessed) and 'gha'ib' (absent).
2
Understand the initial response
The author replies by giving examples of what would be truly foolish for God, but clarifies that God does not will such things.
The example of Pharaoh shows that God's will is consistent with His knowledge.
3
Learn the Ash'ari definition
Al-Ash'ari defines foolishness as what is prohibited. Since there is no prohibition on God, He cannot be foolish.
This definition resolves the issue by focusing on the presence of prohibition.
4
Reflect on the shahid/gha'ib distinction
The key move is that analogy from witnessed to absent is invalid without a common basis. The Ash'ari response uses this distinction.
This is a common method in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما شبهتهم المعقولة فقولهم : إن مريد السفه سفيه اعتبارا بالشاهد. قلنا: إرادة ما لو كان لأوجب زوال ربوبيته، وإرادة أن لا يكون ما لو لم يكن لزالت ألوهيته ،سفه، وقد مر أنه لو أراد إيمان فرعون فكان ولم يرد كفره فلم يكن مع أنه علم أن كفره يكون وإيمانه لا يكون لكانت إرادة تجهيل نفسه وهو سفه. ثم نقول : السفه عند الأشعري وأهل الحديث ما نهى عنه، وفي الشاهد مريد السفه منهي عنه فكان به سفيهًا، وفي الغائب لا نهي فلم يكن سفيها، وعندنا السفه ما لم يتعلق به عاقبة حميدة فلو تعلقت بإرادة السفه لما كانت سفها كما لم يكن من ابن آدم عليه السلام بقوله: ﴿ إِنِّي أُرِيدُ أَن تَبُوا بِإِثْمِي وَإِيْمَكَ ﴾ سفها وإن كانت إرادة سفه، ولم يكن من موسى عليه السلام حيث قال: (وَاشْدُدْ عَلَى قُلُوبِهِمْ فَلَا يُؤْمِنُوا) سفها فكذا من الله تعالى، وكذا موسى عليه السلام حيث قال للسحرة (بَلْ القُوا) وقال أَلْقُوا مَا أَنتُم مُلْقُونَ أكان ذلك الإلقاء سفها أم حكمة فإن قالوا: كان فجعلوا معارضة الرسول في المعجزة بما يأتيه الساحر من المخرقة حكمة وهو كفر، وإن قالوا : كان الإلقاء سفهًا، قلنا: وهل أراد موسى وجود الإلقاء؟ فإن قالوا: لا. فقد زعموا أنه لم يرد ظهور حجته وإبطال ما يعارضون به حجته وأرادوا بقاء أمر رسالته في حيز الإشكال والتردد، وإن قالوا: نعم. فقد أقروا أنه أراد السفه ولم يصر به سفيها لما كان تحت إرادته ظهور حجته وغلبة دلائله، وفيما نحن فيه يكون تحت إرادة السفه تحقق علمه وخبره فيكون حكمة لا سفها. ويقال لهم: لو أن رسولاً يخبر قومه أن فلانا الكافر يجيئني فيشتمني ويقصد قتلي أيريد تحقيق ذلك ليظهر صدقة في إخباره عن الغيب فيكون معجزة له أم يريد أن لا يوجد ذلك فيظهر كذبه ولا تتحقق معجزته؟ فإن قالوا: بالأول. فقد أقروا أن مريد السفه لا يكون سفيهًا إذا كانت لذلك عاقبة حميدة، وإن قالوا بالثاني ظهر للناس تعنتهم ومكابرتهم، وما قالوا إن مريد سفه نفسه في الشاهد سفيه، فقد سبق جوابه في مسألة خلق الأفعال على أن كثيرًا من أصحابنا لا يطلقون أنه أراد شتم
As for their rational argument, it is their saying: ‘The one who wills foolishness is foolish, by analogy to the witnessed.’ We say: Willing what, if it occurred, would necessitate the removal of His lordship, and willing that what, if it did not occur, would remove His divinity, is foolishness. And it has passed that if He willed the faith of Pharaoh and it occurred, and He did not will his disbelief and it did not occur, while He knew that his disbelief would occur and his faith would not occur, then that would be willing to make Himself ignorant, which is foolishness. Then we say: Foolishness according to al-Ash'ari and the people of hadith is whatever is prohibited. In the witnessed, the one who wills foolishness is prohibited from it, so he becomes foolish by it. In the absent, there is no prohibition, so He is not foolish. According to us, foolishness is that which is not attached to a praiseworthy consequence. So if it is attached to the will of foolishness, it would not be foolishness, just as it was not foolishness from the son of Adam (peace be upon him) in his saying: ‘I intend that you bear my sin and your sin’ (Quran 5:29), even though it was a will of foolishness. And it was not foolishness from Moses (peace be upon him) when he said: ‘And harden their hearts so that they do not believe’ (Quran 10:88). So similarly from Allah Almighty. And likewise, Moses (peace be upon him) when he said to the magicians: ‘Rather, you throw’ and said: ‘Throw what you are going to throw’ – was that throwing foolishness or wisdom? If they say: it was, then they make the opponent of the messenger in the miracle by what the magician brings of deception to be wisdom, which is disbelief. And if they say: the throwing was foolishness, we say: Did Moses will the occurrence of the throwing? If they say: no, then they claim that he did not will the appearance of his proof and the invalidation of what they oppose his proof with, and they willed the state of his messengership to remain in the realm of ambiguity and hesitation. And if they say: yes, then they admit that he willed foolishness and was not made foolish by it because under his will was the appearance of his proof and the dominance of his evidences. In our case, under the will of foolishness is the realization of His knowledge and His report, so it becomes wisdom, not foolishness. And it is said to them: If a messenger informs his people that such-and-such a disbeliever will come to me, insult me, and intend to kill me, does he will the realization of that in order to show his truthfulness in his report about the unseen, making it a miracle for him, or does he will that it not occur so that his lie becomes apparent and his miracle is not realized? If they say: the first, then they admit that the one who wills foolishness is not foolish if it has a praiseworthy consequence. And if they say: the second, then their obstinacy and stubbornness appear to people. As for their statement that the one who wills foolishness for himself in the witnessed is foolish, its answer has already preceded in the issue of the creation of acts, and many of our companions do not apply that He willed insult
Source pages p. 981

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazila's rational argument that 'one who wills foolishness is foolish,' based on analogy from human experience. The author first presents the objection and then responds by defining the terms differently. The author notes that willing something that would negate divine lordship or divinity is indeed foolish, but that is not the case for God. He then discusses the example of Pharaoh: if God had willed Pharaoh's faith while knowing it would not happen, that would be self-deception and foolishness – but God does not will such contradictions. Next, the author distinguishes between definitions of foolishness: al-Ash'ari and Ahl al-Hadith define it as what is prohibited; since there is no prohibition on God, He cannot be foolish. The Maturidi definition (according to the author) is that foolishness is whatever does not have a praiseworthy outcome. Several Quranic examples are given: Adam's son's statement 'I intend that you bear my sin' (5:29) is a will of foolishness but not considered foolish because it aimed at a good consequence (avoiding further sin). Similarly, Moses' prayer to harden Pharaoh's heart (10:88) was not foolish because it was a request for justice and manifestation of power. The author then uses the story of Moses and the magicians: Moses told them to throw first, which seemed to allow showmanship, but it led to the demonstration of the true miracle. If this is foolishness, then it would be wisdom because it resulted in the proof of prophethood. The author further argues that if Moses willed that throwing, it was for a wise purpose, so he was not foolish. Similarly, God's will for evil deeds can have wise outcomes such as the realization of His knowledge and the fulfillment of His decree. Finally, a hypothetical scenario is presented: a prophet predicts a disbeliever's insult; if he wills it to happen to confirm his prophecy, that is wise, not foolish. This supports the point that the outcome determines foolishness, not the mere act of willing. The passage concludes by noting that many Maturidi scholars do not even apply the term 'will' to God's decree of evil, but rather speak of it as His creation.

Key Terms
سفه
إرادة
شاهد
غائب
نهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That willing evil necessarily makes one evil or foolish.
Correction: The Ash'ari response shows that foolishness is defined by prohibition, not by the act of willing itself. Since God is not subject to prohibition, He cannot be foolish.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari definition in this passage, what is foolishness?
A) Anything that has a bad outcome.
B) Whatever is prohibited.
C) Whatever is willed by a human.
D) Anything that contradicts wisdom.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's analogy from human experience fails because God is not subject to prohibition; therefore, the Ash'ari definition of foolishness as prohibited acts exonerates God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself judging God's actions as if He were bound by the same rules as humans? What does this lesson teach you about the danger of comparing God to yourself? Ponder a specific time when you felt something God ordained was 'foolish'. How does the Ash'ari definition challenge that feeling?
?
The Mu'tazila assumed that willing foolishness is always foolish, even for God. Are there any areas of your life where you assume God must think or act like you do? Write down one such assumption and then re-read the source to see how it refutes it. What can you do today to surrender that assumption?
?
If foolishness is defined by prohibition, then God, who is not prohibited, cannot be foolish. This means everything God wills is beyond the category of foolishness. Does your heart fully accept that? Or do you secretly believe some of God's decrees are senseless? Take a moment to confess any doubt and ask for certainty.

The Maturidi Definition and Prophetic Examples
Presents the Maturidi definition and multiple prophetic examples to refute the Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi definition of foolishness as that which lacks a praiseworthy consequence, and how this is supported by examples from the prophets.

Core Idea
Foolishness is defined by the absence of a good outcome, not by the nature of the act willed.

Having refuted the Mu'tazila using the Ash'ari definition, the author now presents the Maturidi position: foolishness is that which has no praiseworthy consequence. This definition allows that even willing what seems foolish can be wise if it leads to a good outcome. The lesson explores prophetic examples, including the son of Adam and Moses, to illustrate this principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the Maturidi definition
Read the line: 'وعندنا السفه ما لم يتعلق به عاقبة حميدة'. Note the emphasis on consequence.
This is the core of the Maturidi position.
2
Examine the example of Adam's son
The son of Adam said 'I intend that you bear my sin' which is a will of foolishness, but it was not considered foolish because it had a good intention (avoiding sin).
The act is judged by its outcome.
3
Examine the example of Moses
Moses prayed for Pharaoh's heart to be hardened and later told the magicians to throw first. Both seem foolish but had wise outcomes.
Moses' actions were part of a divine plan.
4
Understand the hypothetical scenario
A prophet predicts a disbeliever's insult; if he wills it to happen to confirm his prophecy, that is wise.
This reinforces the principle of praiseworthy consequence.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
The author concludes that God's will for evil deeds can be wise because it fulfills His knowledge and decree.
This answers the Mu'tazila objection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a will for something harmful or foolish always makes the willer foolish.
Correction: The Maturidi definition shows that the outcome matters; if a praiseworthy consequence follows, the will is wise, not foolish.

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidi definition in this passage, what makes an act not foolish?
A) It is willed by a prophet.
B) It leads to a praiseworthy consequence.
C) It is prohibited by law.
D) It is done in private.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi school defines foolishness by the absence of a good outcome, allowing that even willing apparent evils can be wise if they serve a greater good.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a decision you made that others considered foolish but you believed would lead to a good outcome. How did you justify it? Does this lesson's criterion of praiseworthy consequence validate your reasoning or expose a flaw?
?
When you reflect on your intentions, do you ever hide behind 'good outcomes' to excuse actions that are clearly harmful? What concrete step can you take today to align your actions with wisdom as defined by praiseworthy consequences?
?
The prophets willed what appeared foolish to achieve a greater good. In what area of your life are you unwilling to risk appearing foolish for the sake of a higher purpose? What is holding you back?

The Principle of Praiseworthy Outcome
Nasafi states the principle of praiseworthy consequence with two prophetic examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the principle that an intention of folly (sufh) is not considered foolish if it leads to a praiseworthy consequence, as demonstrated by examples from Adam and Moses.

Core Idea
Folly is defined by its outcome, not its immediate content.

Sanusi begins by addressing a common objection: how can God will evil without being foolish? He introduces a key principle—an act is judged by its ultimate consequence, not its immediate appearance. The examples of Adam's son and Moses show that even intentions involving evil can be wise if they serve a greater good. This lesson unpacks that foundational idea.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the rhythm. Then read the English translation. Notice the two examples used: the son of Adam and Moses.
The Quranic verses are quoted directly—reflect on their context.
2
Identify the core principle
Find the sentence that states the principle: 'What does not have a praiseworthy consequence... would not be folly.' Write it down in your own words.
3
Analyze each example
For each example (Adam's son and Moses), ask: What was the apparent folly? What was the praiseworthy consequence? Why does Nasafi say it was not actually foolish?
The praiseworthy consequence in Moses' case is the manifestation of God's signs.
4
Apply to the divine context
Nasafi concludes 'thus also from God Almighty.' Reflect on how this principle removes the accusation of foolishness from God when He wills events that seem evil.

Source Text

ما لم يتعلق به عاقبة حميدة فلو تعلقت بإرادة السفه لما كانت سفها كما لم يكن من ابن آدم عليه السلام بقوله: ﴿ إِنِّي أُرِيدُ أَن تَبُوا بِإِثْمِي وَإِيْمَكَ ﴾ سفها وإن كانت إرادة سفه، ولم يكن من موسى عليه السلام حيث قال: ﴿ وَاشْدُدْ عَلَى قُلُوبِهِمْ فَلَا يُؤْمِنُوا ﴾ سفها فكذا من الله تعالى، وكذا موسى عليه السلام حيث قال للسحرة ﴿ بَلْ القُوا ﴾ وقال ﴿ أَلْقُوا مَا أَنتُم مُلْقُونَ ﴾ أكان ذلك الإلقاء سفها أم حكمة (فإن قالوا: كان فجعلوا معارضة الرسول في المعجزة بما يأتيه الساحر من المخرقة حكمة) حكمة وهو كفر، وإن قالوا : كان الإلقاء سفهًا، قلنا: وهل أراد موسى وجود الإلقاء؟ فإن قالوا: لا. فقد زعموا أنه لم يرد ظهور حجته وإبطال ما يعارضون به حجته وأرادوا بقاء أمر رسالته في حيز الإشكال والتردد، وإن قالوا: نعم. فقد أقروا أنه أراد السفه ولم يصر به سفيها لما كان تحت إرادته ظهور حجته وغلبة دلائله، وفيما نحن فيه يكون تحت إرادة السفه تحقق علمه وخبره فيكون حكمة لا سفها. ويقال لهم: لو أن رسولاً يخبر قومه أن فلانا الكافر يجيئني فيشتمني ويقصد قتلي أيريد تحقيق ذلك ليظهر صدقة في إخباره عن الغيب فيكون معجزة له أم يريد أن لا يوجد ذلك فيظهر كذبه ولا تتحقق معجزته؟ فإن قالوا: بالأول. فقد أقروا أن مريد السفه لا يكون سفيهًا إذا كانت لذلك عاقبة حميدة، وإن قالوا بالثاني ظهر للناس تعنتهم ومكابرتهم، وما قالوا إن مريد سفه نفسه في الشاهد سفيه، فقد سبق جوابه في مسألة خلق الأفعال على أن كثيرًا من أصحابنا لا يطلقون أنه أراد شتم
قالوا: نعم - وهو قولهم - قيل : والكفر قبيح لعينه فإذا الله قبحه فقد أراد عينه إذ وهذا ما أنكرتموه ، وإن قالوا لا يريد أن يكون قبيحًا ، فقد أراد أن يكون من مذهبهم أن ما ليس بقبيح فهو حسن ولهذا زعموا أن كل مباح حسن، وزعموا أن الوجوب أو الندب يقتضي حسنًا زائدًا على أصل الحسن، والقول بأن الكفر حسن كفر صريح، وما يقولون : إن العبد لا يمكنه الخروج عن إرادة الله تعالى فيصير مجبوراً. قلنا : ونعلم يقينًا أنه ليس بمجبور ، وهذا لأنه يريد أن يوجد فعله الاختياري لا الاضطراري فيخرج اختياريًا لا اضطراريًا كما أراد. ثم يقال : والعبد لا يمكنه الخروج عن علم الله تعالى فيكون مجبوراً فإن قال: نعم. أبطل مذهبه وارتكب محالاً، وإن قال : لا ، أبطل شبهته، وكذا الله تعالى لا يخرج عن معلومه وليس بمضطر لما علم أنه يفعل ما يفعل باختياره فلم يكن هوا مضطرًا ولا العبد ، فكذا إذا أراد أن العبد يفعل ما يفعل باختياره لم يصر مضطرًا والله الموفق. (وقولهم : إن إرادة ما لا يرضى به سفه فقد سبق عنه الجواب) وكذا قولهم: إن الأمر بما لا يريد ،سفه، فقد سبق عنه الجواب. ثم نقول: إن رجلاً من حكماء البشر لو كان له عبد عاص متمرد لا يطيعه فيما يأمره ولا ينزجر عما يزجره، فأخذ في تأديبه وتثقيفه، فرآه بعض أصحابه أو
What does not have a praiseworthy consequence - for if it were attached to an intention of folly [sufh] it would not be folly, just as it was not folly from the son of Adam, peace be upon him, in his saying: 'I intend that you bear my sin and your sin,' even though it was an intention of folly; and it was not folly from Moses, peace be upon him, when he said: 'And harden their hearts so that they do not believe'; thus also from God Almighty. Similarly, Moses, peace be upon him, when he said to the sorcerers: 'Rather, throw!' and said: 'Throw what you are throwing!' Was that throwing folly or wisdom? If they say: 'It was folly,' then they have made opposing the messenger in the miracle by what the sorcerer brings of trickery wisdom - (wisdom) and that is disbelief. If they say: 'The throwing was folly,' we say: Did Moses intend the occurrence of the throwing? If they say: No, then they claim he did not intend the manifestation of his proof and the invalidation of what they oppose his proof with, and they intended his mission to remain in a state of ambiguity and hesitation. If they say: Yes, then they admitted that he intended folly but did not become foolish by it, because under his intention was the manifestation of his proof and the dominance of his evidences. In our case, under the intention of folly is the realization of His knowledge and information, so it becomes wisdom, not folly. And it is said to them: If a messenger informs his people that a certain disbeliever will come to me and insult me and intend to kill me, does he intend the realization of that to show his truthfulness in his report of the unseen, making it a miracle for him, or does he intend that it not occur so that his lie appears and his miracle is not realized? If they say: The first, then they admit that the one who intends folly is not a fool if there is a praiseworthy consequence for it. If they say: The second, then their obstinacy and stubbornness appear to people. As for their saying that one who intends his own folly in the witnessed is a fool, its answer has already preceded in the issue of the creation of acts, and that many of our companions do not affirm that He intended insult. They said: Yes - and that is their statement - it was said: Disbelief is evil in itself, so since God made it evil, He intended it itself, for this is what you denied. If they say: He does not intend it to be evil, then He intended it to be - according to their doctrine - what is not evil is good, therefore they claim that every permissible act is good, and they claim that obligation or recommendation entails an additional goodness beyond the original goodness. The statement that disbelief is good is outright disbelief. As for their saying: The servant cannot exit from God's will, so he becomes compelled. We say: And we know with certainty that he is not compelled, and this is because he intends his voluntary act to exist, not the involuntary one; so it emerges as voluntary, not involuntary, as He intended. Then it is said: And the servant cannot exit from God's knowledge, so he becomes compelled. If he says: Yes, he invalidates his doctrine and commits an absurdity. If he says: No, he invalidates his sophism. Likewise, God Almighty does not exit from His known, nor is He compelled, because He knows that He does what He does by His choice, so He is not compelled, nor is the servant. Thus, if He intends that the servant does what he does by his choice, he does not become compelled. And God is the Grantor of success. (And their saying: The intention of what He is not pleased with is folly - its answer has already preceded.) Likewise their saying: Commanding what He does not intend is folly - its answer has already preceded. Then we say: If a man from the wise of humans had a rebellious, disobedient slave who does not obey him in what he commands nor desists from what he forbids, and he began to discipline and reform him, and one of his companions saw him or...
Source pages p. 982

Commentary
This passage is a continuous refutation of several objections against God's will concerning evil. Nasafi proceeds by answering each objection in turn. First, he addresses the claim that if God wills something foolish (sufh) He would be foolish. Nasafi responds that an act is not deemed foolish if it leads to a praiseworthy outcome. He gives examples: Adam's son (Cain) intended to bear his brother's sin, and Moses asked for Pharaoh's heart to be hardened to manifest God's signs. Both intentions involve evil but serve a greater good, so they are not foolish. Next, Nasafi analyzes the incident of Moses and the sorcerers: Moses commanded them to throw their staffs, which was an action with two possible interpretations. If the opponents claim it was folly, they must explain why Moses willed it. They cannot deny Moses intended the throwing, and if they admit he willed it but still call it folly, Nasafi argues that because the throwing led to the victory of truth, it was actually wisdom. Nasafi then applies this principle to God: God's will for evil events is not foolish because it leads to the realization of His knowledge and prophecy, thus it is wisdom. Nasafi reinforces this with an analogy of a messenger who predicts a disbeliever will insult him. If the messenger wills that event to confirm his truthfulness, it is not foolish because it serves a good end. Moving to a new objection, Nasafi addresses disbelief (kufr) itself: the opponents argue that disbelief is evil in itself, so if God wills it, He wills evil. Nasafi counters that this leads to absurdity: either God wills disbelief as evil, which they deny, or He wills it not as evil, which implies that disbelief could be good – a manifest heresy. Then Nasafi tackles the objection of compulsion (jabr): if God wills all actions, the servant is compelled. Nasafi replies that the servant wills his own acts voluntarily; God wills that the servant act by his choice, not by force. The same applies to God's knowledge: knowing an act does not compel it. Finally, Nasafi gives a human analogy: a wise master disciplines a rebellious slave for reform, and observers might misunderstand. The master's will for punishment is not foolish because it aims at correction. The passage ends mid-analogy.

Key Terms
الإرادة (al-irāda)
السفه (al-sufh)
الحكمة (al-ḥikma)
العاقبة الحميدة (al-ʿāqiba al-ḥamīda)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's will for evil events (e.g., disbelief) makes Him foolish or unjust.
Correction: Nasafi argues that if the evil event has a praiseworthy consequence, the intention is not foolish, as shown by prophetic examples.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, why is Moses' prayer to harden Pharaoh's hearts not considered foolish?
A) Because Moses did not actually mean it.
B) Because it had a praiseworthy consequence (manifestation of God's signs).
C) Because Pharaoh deserved it.
D) Because God forced Moses to say it.

Key Takeaway
An intention is judged by its ultimate consequence; God's will for apparent evil is wise because it serves praiseworthy ends.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you judged a difficult event in your life as foolish or unfair, only later to see God's wisdom in it? How can you train yourself to trust the praiseworthy outcome even before you see it?
?
The prophets' intentions that appeared evil were not foolish because of their ultimate purpose. In what areas of your life are you quick to label others' actions as foolish without considering the bigger picture? What would it take for you to pause and seek the praiseworthy outcome first?
?
If God's will for apparent evil is always wise, what does that demand of your heart when you face trials? Will you commit to affirming His wisdom even when you cannot see the good, as the prophets did?

7 min
The Case of Moses and the Sorcerers
Nasafi analyzes the Moses-sorcerers incident to demonstrate wisdom in apparent folly.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the incident of Moses and the sorcerers illustrates that an action can be intended for a wise purpose even if it appears foolish.

Core Idea
Moses' command to the sorcerers was wisdom, not folly, because it led to the triumph of truth.

Nasafi now deepens the argument by analyzing a specific prophetic event: Moses telling the sorcerers to throw their staffs. On the surface, commanding a rival magician seems counterproductive. But Nasafi shows that this very act became the means by which Moses' prophethood was vindicated. This lesson walks through the dialectical reasoning that proves the action was wisdom, not folly.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the question Nasafi poses: 'Was that throwing folly or wisdom?'
2
Follow the dialectical exchange
Nasafi presents two possible answers from opponents. Identify each: 1) They call it wisdom, which implies that opposing a miracle with trickery is wisdom (a contradiction). 2) They call it folly. Then trace Nasafi's response to each.
Pay attention to the hypothetical 'if they say' statements.
3
Understand the key argument
When opponents say the throwing was folly, Nasafi asks: Did Moses intend the throwing? If they say no, they deny Moses intended his own proof to manifest. If they say yes, then Moses intended folly but did not become foolish because the outcome was the manifestation of his proof.
This is the core: the outcome redeems the intention.
4
Apply to the divine case
Nasafi concludes that in our discussion of God, under the intention of folly lies the realization of His knowledge, so it becomes wisdom.

Commentary
This passage is a continuous refutation of several objections against God's will concerning evil. Nasafi proceeds by answering each objection in turn. First, he addresses the claim that if God wills something foolish (sufh) He would be foolish. Nasafi responds that an act is not deemed foolish if it leads to a praiseworthy outcome. He gives examples: Adam's son (Cain) intended to bear his brother's sin, and Moses asked for Pharaoh's heart to be hardened to manifest God's signs. Both intentions involve evil but serve a greater good, so they are not foolish. Next, Nasafi analyzes the incident of Moses and the sorcerers: Moses commanded them to throw their staffs, which was an action with two possible interpretations. If the opponents claim it was folly, they must explain why Moses willed it. They cannot deny Moses intended the throwing, and if they admit he willed it but still call it folly, Nasafi argues that because the throwing led to the victory of truth, it was actually wisdom. Nasafi then applies this principle to God: God's will for evil events is not foolish because it leads to the realization of His knowledge and prophecy, thus it is wisdom. Nasafi reinforces this with an analogy of a messenger who predicts a disbeliever will insult him. If the messenger wills that event to confirm his truthfulness, it is not foolish because it serves a good end. Moving to a new objection, Nasafi addresses disbelief (kufr) itself: the opponents argue that disbelief is evil in itself, so if God wills it, He wills evil. Nasafi counters that this leads to absurdity: either God wills disbelief as evil, which they deny, or He wills it not as evil, which implies that disbelief could be good – a manifest heresy. Then Nasafi tackles the objection of compulsion (jabr): if God wills all actions, the servant is compelled. Nasafi replies that the servant wills his own acts voluntarily; God wills that the servant act by his choice, not by force. The same applies to God's knowledge: knowing an act does not compel it. Finally, Nasafi gives a human analogy: a wise master disciplines a rebellious slave for reform, and observers might misunderstand. The master's will for punishment is not foolish because it aims at correction. The passage ends mid-analogy.

المعجزة (al-muʿjiza)
المخرقة (al-makhraqa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Commanding something that appears harmful or counterproductive is always foolish.
Correction: Nasafi shows that the same act can be wise if its ultimate purpose is the manifestation of truth.

Checkpoint
What does Nasafi argue would follow if opponents say the throwing was wisdom?
A) Then they admit Moses was a fool.
B) Then they make opposing a miracle with trickery a wise act, which is disbelief.
C) Then they have no further objection.
D) Then they must deny Moses was a prophet.

Key Takeaway
Moses' command to the sorcerers, though appearing to aid his opponents, was wisdom because it led to the clear manifestation of his prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever questioned God's wisdom when He allowed a situation that seemed to benefit those who oppose the truth? How does Moses' example challenge you to trust the ultimate victory of truth even when appearances suggest otherwise?
?
When you face a trial or see evil in the world, do you accuse God in your heart of folly or doubt His wisdom? Take a moment to repent of such thoughts and affirm that God’s will, even in hardship, is always wise because it serves a greater purpose known to Him alone.
?
If you were in Moses' position, would you have had the courage to seemingly aid the opponents for the sake of a greater plan? What does this passage expose about your level of trust in God’s strategy over your own short-sighted reasoning?

The Messenger Analogy and the Disbelief Objection
Nasafi uses a messenger analogy and a dilemma on disbelief to refute objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two further arguments: the analogy of a messenger predicting an insult, and the refutation that God's will for disbelief entails willing evil.

Core Idea
Willing an evil event for a good purpose is not itself evil; denying this leads to absurdity.

Nasafi continues his refutation with a vivid analogy: a messenger predicts a disbeliever will insult him. If the messenger wills that event to confirm his truthfulness, he is not foolish. Then Nasafi tackles the objection that disbelief is evil in itself, so God willing it would be evil. He shows the opponents' logic leads to the heretical conclusion that disbelief could be good. This lesson covers both arguments, which reinforce each other.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation for both the messenger analogy and the disbelief objection. Notice the shift from analogy to direct theological argument.
2
Analyze the messenger analogy
The messenger predicts an insult. Ask: what would the messenger want? If he wants the insult to occur to prove his truthfulness, is he foolish? Nasafi says no, because of the praiseworthy outcome (confirmation of his prophethood).
3
Trace the opponents' dilemma on disbelief
Opponents say disbelief is evil in itself, so if God wills it, He wills evil. Nasafi presents a dilemma: either God wills disbelief as evil (which they deny) or He wills it not as evil, implying disbelief could be good.
This forces the opponent into either contradiction or heresy.
4
Connect the two arguments
Both arguments show that the moral status of an intention depends on its context and consequence, not just its immediate object.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما لم يتعلق به عاقبة حميدة فلو تعلقت بإرادة السفه لما كانت سفها كما لم يكن من ابن آدم عليه السلام بقوله: ﴿ إِنِّي أُرِيدُ أَن تَبُوا بِإِثْمِي وَإِيْمَكَ ﴾ سفها وإن كانت إرادة سفه، ولم يكن من موسى عليه السلام حيث قال: ﴿ وَاشْدُدْ عَلَى قُلُوبِهِمْ فَلَا يُؤْمِنُوا ﴾ سفها فكذا من الله تعالى، وكذا موسى عليه السلام حيث قال للسحرة ﴿ بَلْ القُوا ﴾ وقال ﴿ أَلْقُوا مَا أَنتُم مُلْقُونَ ﴾ أكان ذلك الإلقاء سفها أم حكمة (فإن قالوا: كان فجعلوا معارضة الرسول في المعجزة بما يأتيه الساحر من المخرقة حكمة) حكمة وهو كفر، وإن قالوا : كان الإلقاء سفهًا، قلنا: وهل أراد موسى وجود الإلقاء؟ فإن قالوا: لا. فقد زعموا أنه لم يرد ظهور حجته وإبطال ما يعارضون به حجته وأرادوا بقاء أمر رسالته في حيز الإشكال والتردد، وإن قالوا: نعم. فقد أقروا أنه أراد السفه ولم يصر به سفيها لما كان تحت إرادته ظهور حجته وغلبة دلائله، وفيما نحن فيه يكون تحت إرادة السفه تحقق علمه وخبره فيكون حكمة لا سفها. ويقال لهم: لو أن رسولاً يخبر قومه أن فلانا الكافر يجيئني فيشتمني ويقصد قتلي أيريد تحقيق ذلك ليظهر صدقة في إخباره عن الغيب فيكون معجزة له أم يريد أن لا يوجد ذلك فيظهر كذبه ولا تتحقق معجزته؟ فإن قالوا: بالأول. فقد أقروا أن مريد السفه لا يكون سفيهًا إذا كانت لذلك عاقبة حميدة، وإن قالوا بالثاني ظهر للناس تعنتهم ومكابرتهم، وما قالوا إن مريد سفه نفسه في الشاهد سفيه، فقد سبق جوابه في مسألة خلق الأفعال على أن كثيرًا من أصحابنا لا يطلقون أنه أراد شتم
قالوا: نعم - وهو قولهم - قيل : والكفر قبيح لعينه فإذا الله قبحه فقد أراد عينه إذ وهذا ما أنكرتموه ، وإن قالوا لا يريد أن يكون قبيحًا ، فقد أراد أن يكون من مذهبهم أن ما ليس بقبيح فهو حسن ولهذا زعموا أن كل مباح حسن، وزعموا أن الوجوب أو الندب يقتضي حسنًا زائدًا على أصل الحسن، والقول بأن الكفر حسن كفر صريح، وما يقولون : إن العبد لا يمكنه الخروج عن إرادة الله تعالى فيصير مجبوراً. قلنا : ونعلم يقينًا أنه ليس بمجبور ، وهذا لأنه يريد أن يوجد فعله الاختياري لا الاضطراري فيخرج اختياريًا لا اضطراريًا كما أراد. ثم يقال : والعبد لا يمكنه الخروج عن علم الله تعالى فيكون مجبوراً فإن قال: نعم. أبطل مذهبه وارتكب محالاً، وإن قال : لا ، أبطل شبهته، وكذا الله تعالى لا يخرج عن معلومه وليس بمضطر لما علم أنه يفعل ما يفعل باختياره فلم يكن هوا مضطرًا ولا العبد ، فكذا إذا أراد أن العبد يفعل ما يفعل باختياره لم يصر مضطرًا والله الموفق. (وقولهم : إن إرادة ما لا يرضى به سفه فقد سبق عنه الجواب) وكذا قولهم: إن الأمر بما لا يريد ،سفه، فقد سبق عنه الجواب. ثم نقول: إن رجلاً من حكماء البشر لو كان له عبد عاص متمرد لا يطيعه فيما يأمره ولا ينزجر عما يزجره، فأخذ في تأديبه وتثقيفه، فرآه بعض أصحابه أو
What does not have a praiseworthy consequence - for if it were attached to an intention of folly [sufh] it would not be folly, just as it was not folly from the son of Adam, peace be upon him, in his saying: 'I intend that you bear my sin and your sin,' even though it was an intention of folly; and it was not folly from Moses, peace be upon him, when he said: 'And harden their hearts so that they do not believe'; thus also from God Almighty. Similarly, Moses, peace be upon him, when he said to the sorcerers: 'Rather, throw!' and said: 'Throw what you are throwing!' Was that throwing folly or wisdom? If they say: 'It was folly,' then they have made opposing the messenger in the miracle by what the sorcerer brings of trickery wisdom - (wisdom) and that is disbelief. If they say: 'The throwing was folly,' we say: Did Moses intend the occurrence of the throwing? If they say: No, then they claim he did not intend the manifestation of his proof and the invalidation of what they oppose his proof with, and they intended his mission to remain in a state of ambiguity and hesitation. If they say: Yes, then they admitted that he intended folly but did not become foolish by it, because under his intention was the manifestation of his proof and the dominance of his evidences. In our case, under the intention of folly is the realization of His knowledge and information, so it becomes wisdom, not folly. And it is said to them: If a messenger informs his people that a certain disbeliever will come to me and insult me and intend to kill me, does he intend the realization of that to show his truthfulness in his report of the unseen, making it a miracle for him, or does he intend that it not occur so that his lie appears and his miracle is not realized? If they say: The first, then they admit that the one who intends folly is not a fool if there is a praiseworthy consequence for it. If they say: The second, then their obstinacy and stubbornness appear to people. As for their saying that one who intends his own folly in the witnessed is a fool, its answer has already preceded in the issue of the creation of acts, and that many of our companions do not affirm that He intended insult. They said: Yes - and that is their statement - it was said: Disbelief is evil in itself, so since God made it evil, He intended it itself, for this is what you denied. If they say: He does not intend it to be evil, then He intended it to be - according to their doctrine - what is not evil is good, therefore they claim that every permissible act is good, and they claim that obligation or recommendation entails an additional goodness beyond the original goodness. The statement that disbelief is good is outright disbelief. As for their saying: The servant cannot exit from God's will, so he becomes compelled. We say: And we know with certainty that he is not compelled, and this is because he intends his voluntary act to exist, not the involuntary one; so it emerges as voluntary, not involuntary, as He intended. Then it is said: And the servant cannot exit from God's knowledge, so he becomes compelled. If he says: Yes, he invalidates his doctrine and commits an absurdity. If he says: No, he invalidates his sophism. Likewise, God Almighty does not exit from His known, nor is He compelled, because He knows that He does what He does by His choice, so He is not compelled, nor is the servant. Thus, if He intends that the servant does what he does by his choice, he does not become compelled. And God is the Grantor of success. (And their saying: The intention of what He is not pleased with is folly - its answer has already preceded.) Likewise their saying: Commanding what He does not intend is folly - its answer has already preceded. Then we say: If a man from the wise of humans had a rebellious, disobedient slave who does not obey him in what he commands nor desists from what he forbids, and he began to discipline and reform him, and one of his companions saw him or...
Source pages p. 982

Commentary
This passage is a continuous refutation of several objections against God's will concerning evil. Nasafi proceeds by answering each objection in turn. First, he addresses the claim that if God wills something foolish (sufh) He would be foolish. Nasafi responds that an act is not deemed foolish if it leads to a praiseworthy outcome. He gives examples: Adam's son (Cain) intended to bear his brother's sin, and Moses asked for Pharaoh's heart to be hardened to manifest God's signs. Both intentions involve evil but serve a greater good, so they are not foolish. Next, Nasafi analyzes the incident of Moses and the sorcerers: Moses commanded them to throw their staffs, which was an action with two possible interpretations. If the opponents claim it was folly, they must explain why Moses willed it. They cannot deny Moses intended the throwing, and if they admit he willed it but still call it folly, Nasafi argues that because the throwing led to the victory of truth, it was actually wisdom. Nasafi then applies this principle to God: God's will for evil events is not foolish because it leads to the realization of His knowledge and prophecy, thus it is wisdom. Nasafi reinforces this with an analogy of a messenger who predicts a disbeliever will insult him. If the messenger wills that event to confirm his truthfulness, it is not foolish because it serves a good end. Moving to a new objection, Nasafi addresses disbelief (kufr) itself: the opponents argue that disbelief is evil in itself, so if God wills it, He wills evil. Nasafi counters that this leads to absurdity: either God wills disbelief as evil, which they deny, or He wills it not as evil, which implies that disbelief could be good – a manifest heresy. Then Nasafi tackles the objection of compulsion (jabr): if God wills all actions, the servant is compelled. Nasafi replies that the servant wills his own acts voluntarily; God wills that the servant act by his choice, not by force. The same applies to God's knowledge: knowing an act does not compel it. Finally, Nasafi gives a human analogy: a wise master disciplines a rebellious slave for reform, and observers might misunderstand. The master's will for punishment is not foolish because it aims at correction. The passage ends mid-analogy.

Key Terms
الكفر (al-kufr)
القبيح (al-qabīḥ)
الحسن (al-ḥasan)
المعجزة (al-muʿjiza)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God wills disbelief, He must will evil, which is impossible for a good God.
Correction: Nasafi shows that if God does not will it as evil, then the action loses its evil quality in that context, which the opponents cannot accept without heresy.

Checkpoint
What does Nasafi conclude if opponents say the messenger intends the insult to occur?
A) The messenger is foolish.
B) They admit that intending folly for a praiseworthy outcome is not foolish.
C) The messenger is lying.
D) The opponents are correct.

Key Takeaway
Both the messenger analogy and the disbelief dilemma show that intentions are judged by their outcomes and that God's will for disbelief does not entail willing evil as evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a painful situation that you know God has willed, do you ever doubt His goodness? How does the messenger's willingness to endure insult for a greater purpose challenge you to trust God's wisdom even when you cannot see the full picture? What would it take for you to truly believe that every trial He wills has a praiseworthy consequence?
?
Think of a time when you intended something that seemed wrong to others but was for a good outcome. Did you feel justified? Now consider that God's intentions are infinitely higher. Are you willing to surrender your judgment of His actions and admit that your limited perspective cannot grasp His purposes? What specific area of your life do you need to surrender today?
?
The passage warns that calling disbelief 'good' is outright disbelief. Have you ever rationalised a sin or a partner's sin by focusing on its apparent 'good' side? How does this lesson force you to re-evaluate that rationalisation? Commit to one practical step to avoid calling what God has declared evil, 'good' in your thoughts or words.

Divine Attributes
intermediate
6 min
Refuting Compulsion from God's Will and Knowledge
Nasafi refutes the objection of compulsion from divine will and knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the objection that God's will for all actions compels humans, using the distinction between voluntary and involuntary acts.

Core Idea
God's will that a person acts voluntarily does not compel them; it respects their choice.

The final objection Nasafi addresses is that if God wills every action, then humans are compelled (jabr). He responds by clarifying that God wills human actions as voluntary, not involuntary. The same logic applies to God's knowledge: knowing an act does not cause it. This lesson unpacks this subtle but crucial distinction that preserves human responsibility while affirming divine will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation for the compulsion objection and its refutation. Notice the careful wording about 'voluntary' vs 'involuntary' acts.
2
Identify the opponents' claim
Opponents say: 'The servant cannot exit from God's will, so he becomes compelled.' Understand why this seems logical.
3
Understand Nasafi's response
Nasafi says we know with certainty that the servant is not compelled because the servant intends his own act as voluntary. God wills that the act be voluntary, not forced.
The key phrase: 'he intends his voluntary act to exist, not the involuntary one.'
4
Apply the same logic to knowledge
Nasafi extends the argument: just as God's knowledge does not make Him compelled, His will does not make the servant compelled. Both are compatible with free choice.

What does not have a praiseworthy consequence - for if it were attached to an intention of folly [sufh] it would not be folly, just as it was not folly from the son of Adam, peace be upon him, in his saying: 'I intend that you bear my sin and your sin,' even though it was an intention of folly; and it was not folly from Moses, peace be upon him, when he said: 'And harden their hearts so that they do not believe'; thus also from God Almighty. Commentary
This passage is a continuous refutation of several objections against God's will concerning evil. Nasafi proceeds by answering each objection in turn. First, he addresses the claim that if God wills something foolish (sufh) He would be foolish. Nasafi responds that an act is not deemed foolish if it leads to a praiseworthy outcome. He gives examples: Adam's son (Cain) intended to bear his brother's sin, and Moses asked for Pharaoh's heart to be hardened to manifest God's signs. Both intentions involve evil but serve a greater good, so they are not foolish. Next, Nasafi analyzes the incident of Moses and the sorcerers: Moses commanded them to throw their staffs, which was an action with two possible interpretations. If the opponents claim it was folly, they must explain why Moses willed it. They cannot deny Moses intended the throwing, and if they admit he willed it but still call it folly, Nasafi argues that because the throwing led to the victory of truth, it was actually wisdom. Nasafi then applies this principle to God: God's will for evil events is not foolish because it leads to the realization of His knowledge and prophecy, thus it is wisdom. Nasafi reinforces this with an analogy of a messenger who predicts a disbeliever will insult him. If the messenger wills that event to confirm his truthfulness, it is not foolish because it serves a good end. Moving to a new objection, Nasafi addresses disbelief (kufr) itself: the opponents argue that disbelief is evil in itself, so if God wills it, He wills evil. Nasafi counters that this leads to absurdity: either God wills disbelief as evil, which they deny, or He wills it not as evil, which implies that disbelief could be good – a manifest heresy. Then Nasafi tackles the objection of compulsion (jabr): if God wills all actions, the servant is compelled. Nasafi replies that the servant wills his own acts voluntarily; God wills that the servant act by his choice, not by force. The same applies to God's knowledge: knowing an act does not compel it. Finally, Nasafi gives a human analogy: a wise master disciplines a rebellious slave for reform, and observers might misunderstand. The master's will for punishment is not foolish because it aims at correction. The passage ends mid-analogy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God wills everything, human actions are forced, removing moral responsibility.
Correction: Nasafi argues that God wills human actions as voluntary, so they remain free and responsible.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, why is the servant not compelled even though God wills his act?
A) Because the servant can resist God's will.
B) Because God wills the act as voluntary, not involuntary.
C) Because God's will is not effective.
D) Because the servant is not aware of God's will.

Key Takeaway
God's will for human actions does not compel them because He wills them to be voluntary, preserving human choice and responsibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you disobey Allah, do you ever find yourself thinking that it was 'destined' or that Allah 'made you do it'? How does Nasafi's argument that Allah wills your act to be voluntary confront that excuse?
?
If you truly believed that Allah's knowledge of your choices does not compel them, how would that change the way you approach repentance and striving to change your habits?
?
Nasafi says we know by certainty that we are not compelled. In your moments of weakness, do you truly feel that certainty? What excuses do you make that contradict this knowledge?

Refuting Compulsion: Divine Will and Human Choice
al-Nasafi refutes the claim that God's will compels human actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that God's will compels human actions is false, and how the distinction between voluntary and involuntary acts resolves the issue.

Core Idea
God's will does not compel; it wills our voluntary acts.

In this passage, the author Imam al-Nasafi refutes a common objection against divine predestination: that if everything happens by God's will, then humans are forced to act. He shows that God wills our actions to be voluntary, and draws a parallel with God's knowledge to demonstrate that foreknowledge does not entail compulsion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: the opponent's claim, the author's response, and the analogy with knowledge.
Pay attention to the key Arabic terms for voluntary and involuntary.
2
Identify the key distinction
Look for the terms 'اختياري' (voluntary) and 'اضطراري' (involuntary). Understand how this distinction refutes the claim of compulsion.
The author argues that God wills the act to be voluntary, so the servant acts voluntarily as willed.
3
Analyze the analogy with knowledge
See how the author uses God's knowledge to argue against compulsion. Consider why the opponent's objection fails: just as knowledge does not compel, will does not compel.
The analogy shows that foreknowledge and will are not constraints on free action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا: نعم - وهو قولهم - قيل : والكفر قبيح لعينه فإذا الله قبحه)(۱) فقد أراد عينه إذ وهذا ما أنكرتموه ، وإن قالوا لا يريد أن يكون قبيحًا ، فقد أراد أن يكون من مذهبهم أن ما ليس بقبيح فهو حسن ولهذا زعموا أن كل مباح حسن، وزعموا أن الوجوب أو الندب يقتضي حسنًا زائدًا على أصل الحسن، والقول بأن(۲) الكفر حسن كفر صريح، وما يقولون : إن العبد لا يمكنه الخروج عن إرادة الله تعالى فيصير مجبوراً. قلنا : ونعلم يقينًا أنه ليس بمجبور ، وهذا لأنه يريد أن يوجد فعله الاختياري لا الاضطراري (۳) فيخرج اختياريًا لا (٤) اضطراريًا كما أراد. ثم يقال : والعبد لا يمكنه الخروج عن علم الله تعالى فيكون مجبوراً فإن قال: نعم. أبطل مذهبه وارتكب محالاً، وإن قال : لا ، أبطل شبهته، وكذا الله تعالى لا يخرج عن معلومه وليس بمضطر لما علم أنه يفعل ما يفعل باختياره فلم يكن هوا مضطرًا ولا العبد والله الموفق. (0) وقولهم : إن إرادة ما لا يرضى به سفه فقد سبق عنه الجواب، وكذا قولهم: إن الأمر بما لا يريد ،سفه، فقد سبق عنه الجواب.
They said: Yes—and that is their statement—it was said: Disbelief is ugly in itself, so if God made it ugly (1), then He willed its essence, and this is what you denied. And if they say He does not will that it be ugly, then He wills that it be from their doctrine that whatever is not ugly is good, and for this they claimed that every permissible thing is good, and they claimed that obligation or recommendation entails an additional goodness beyond the original goodness, and the statement that disbelief is good is outright disbelief. And they say: The servant cannot exit from the will of God Almighty, so he becomes compelled. We said: And we know with certainty that he is not compelled, and this is because He wills to bring about his voluntary act, not the involuntary one (3), so he emerges voluntarily, not involuntarily, as He willed. Then it is said: The servant cannot exit from the knowledge of God Almighty, so he becomes compelled. If he says: Yes, he nullifies his doctrine and commits an absurdity; and if he says: No, he nullifies his sophistry. And likewise, God Almighty does not exit from what He knows, and He is not necessitated because He knows that He does what He does by His choice, so He is not compelled, nor is the servant, and God is the grantor of success. (0) And their statement: Willing what one is not pleased with is foolishness—the answer to it has already preceded. And likewise their statement: Commanding what one does not will is foolishness—the answer to it has already preceded.
Source pages p. 983

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation of the claim that God's will compels human actions. The opponents argue that if disbelief is ugly in itself, and God wills it, then He wills ugliness—which they deny. The author responds by clarifying that the servant acts voluntarily. He then draws an analogy with God's knowledge: just as God's knowledge does not compel, His will does not compel either. The passage also notes that answers to certain objections have already been given.

Key Terms
إرادة (will)
جبر (compulsion)
اختياري (voluntary)
اضطراري (involuntary)
علم الله (God's knowledge)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's will implies that humans are forced to act without choice.
Correction: God wills our actions to be voluntary, so they are not forced. Just as His knowledge does not compel, His will does not compel.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the servant not compelled by God's will?
A) Because God's will is not effective.
B) Because God wills the servant's voluntary act, not an involuntary one.
C) Because the servant is independent of God's will.
D) Because disbelief is not ugly.

Key Takeaway
God's will does not compel human actions because He wills them to be voluntary, just as His knowledge does not compel.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever catch yourself thinking 'It was meant to be' or 'I couldn't help it'? How would al-Nasafi's argument expose that excuse as a denial of your own voluntary involvement?
?
In what areas of your life do you act as if you are compelled rather than choosing responsibly—your anger, your habits, your worship? What would change if you truly believed that God wills your acts to be voluntary?
?
You cannot escape God's will, yet you are not forced. Is this truth a comfort to you or a confrontation? What are you going to do today to take ownership of one decision you have been treating as inevitable?

Distinction Between Qada' and Maqdi
Imam al-Nasafi distinguishes qada' from maqdi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the essential distinction between Allah's decree (qada') and the decreed thing (maqdi), and why the decree itself is always true while the decreed thing may be false.

Core Idea
Qada' is always right; maqdi may be wrong.

Imam al-Nasafi begins by asserting a foundational principle: Allah's decree is inherently true and correct, but the thing decreed—such as disbelief—can be false and evil. This distinction allows Muslims to uphold divine justice without attributing evil to Allah's action. Let's read his opening statement.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the translation. Notice the contrast between 'qada'' and 'maqdi'.
2
Identify the key claim
The author states that disbelief is the decreed thing, not the decree itself. Understand why this matters.
The decree is the act of decreeing; the decreed is the object of that act.
3
Link to the creation of acts
The author references the earlier discussion on the creation of acts. Reflect on how that principle supports this distinction.
This shows the internal consistency of the Maturidi system.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقضاء الله تعالى حق وصواب وبه احتج الكعبي. وعندنا أن الكفر مقضي الله تعالى لا قضاؤه، وقضاؤه حق وصواب، ومقضيه باطل، وقضاء هذا المقضي صواب، لما فيه من الحكمة على ما بينا في مسألة خلق الأفعال، فمن رضي يجعل الله تعالى الكفر باطلاً قبيحًا شرًا فقد رضى بقضاء الله تعالى، ومن لم يرض بذلك فهو غير راض بقضاء الله، ومن رضي بذلك ولم يرض أن يكون الكفر صفة له ولم يحب أن يفعله في نفسه فقد رضي بقضاء الله تعالى ولم يرضى بما يوجب مقته وتعذيبه. واحتج الكعبي بقول النبي عليه السلام: من لم يرض بقضائي ولم يصبر على بلائي فليطلب ربا سواي. وقد بينا أنا رضينا بقضاء الله تعالى وأعلمنا كيفيته على أن حقيقة الخبر في الأمراض والمصائب ألا ترى أن الخلود في النار من قضائه عند المعتزلة إذ لا يثبتون الله تعالى تخليدًا سوى معنى الخلود، فليرض به الكعبي لنفسه، وإلا فليطلب ربا سواه. يحققه أن الكفر عندنا وإن كان بقضاء الله تعالى إلا أن من قضى عليه ذلك یرضی به ويتمسك به تمسكًا لا يرضى بالزوال عنه، وإنما الذي لا يرضى به المقضي عليه ويضجر منه في الأحايين هو الأمراض والمصائب، والمعتزلة لا يرضون بها إلا بعوض فليطلبوا ربًّا سوى من قضى بها عليهم.
And the decree (qada') of Allah Most High is true and correct, and with it al-Ka'bi argued. And according to us, disbelief (kufr) is the decreed thing (maqdi) of Allah Most High, not His decree (qada'), and His decree is true and correct, and its decreed thing is false, and the decree of this decreed thing is correct, due to the wisdom in it, as we explained in the issue of the creation of acts. So whoever is pleased that Allah Most High makes disbelief false, ugly, and evil has indeed been pleased with the decree of Allah Most High, and whoever is not pleased with that is not pleased with the decree of Allah. And whoever is pleased with that but is not pleased that disbelief be an attribute for him and does not love to perform it in himself has been pleased with the decree of Allah Most High and has not been pleased with what necessitates His wrath and punishment. And al-Ka'bi argued with the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him: 'Whoever is not pleased with My decree and does not have patience with My trial, let him seek a lord other than Me.' And we have explained that we are pleased with the decree of Allah Most High and we know its manner, such that the true meaning of the news concerns illnesses and calamities. Do you not see that eternal punishment in the Fire is from His decree according to the Mu'tazila, since they do not affirm for Allah Most High causing to remain eternal except the meaning of remaining eternal. So let al-Ka'bi be pleased with it for himself, otherwise let him seek a lord other than Him. He verifies that disbelief, according to us, even though it is by the decree of Allah Most High, yet the one upon whom that is decreed is pleased with it and clings to it, not being pleased with its removal. Rather, that which the one decreed upon is not pleased with and is distressed by at times is illnesses and calamities, and the Mu'tazila are not pleased with them except with compensation. So let them seek a lord other than the One who decreed them upon them.
Source pages p. 987

Commentary
This passage defines the distinction between qada' (divine decree) and maqdi (the decreed thing). The author begins by stating that Allah's qada' is always true and correct, but the maqdi—such as disbelief—may be false and evil. He clarifies that being pleased with Allah's decree does not require being pleased with disbelief as a personal attribute or action; one can be pleased with the decree for its wisdom while hating the evil of the decreed. The author then addresses the Mu'tazilite al-Ka'bi, who used the hadith 'Whoever is not pleased with My decree...' to argue against the Ash'ari position. The author responds by affirming that they are indeed pleased with Allah's decree, but the hadith refers to patience with trials (illnesses and calamities), not with eternal punishment in Hell. He points out that even al-Ka'bi's own sect (Mu'tazila) holds that the eternal punishment in Hell is from Allah's decree, so al-Ka'bi himself should be pleased with that decree, otherwise he should seek another lord. The author then explains that disbelief, though decreed, is embraced by the disbeliever willingly, not out of compulsion—he is pleased with it and does not want to abandon it. In contrast, illnesses and calamities are disliked by those afflicted, and the Mu'tazila only accept them with compensation. Thus, the hadith about patience applies to trials, not to chosen sins.

Key Terms
قضاء
مقضي
كفر
حكمة
خلق الأفعال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Allah's decree is evil because it includes evil things like disbelief.
Correction: The decree itself is always true and correct; only the decreed thing is false. The wisdom in the decree justifies it.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Nasafi, what is the status of disbelief in relation to qada'?
A) Disbelief is qada' itself.
B) Disbelief is maqdi, not qada'.
C) Disbelief is both qada' and maqdi.
D) Disbelief is neither qada' nor maqdi.

Key Takeaway
Allah's decree is always true; only the decreed thing may be false, and that does not taint the decree's perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness disbelief or evil in the world, does your heart ever question the perfection of Allah's decree? How does the distinction between qada' and maqdi challenge your instinct to blame Allah? What would it take for you to fully trust that His decree is always right, even when the decreed thing seems wrong?
?
The passage says that the decree of disbelief is correct due to wisdom. Think of a personal struggle with faith you have witnessed. Can you bring yourself to believe that Allah's decree behind that struggle contains a wisdom you may never fully grasp? What would change in your life if you truly accepted this?
?
Al-Ka'bi used the correctness of the decree to argue for the correctness of disbelief. Have you ever found yourself rationalizing sin or disbelief by appealing to Allah's decree? How can you guard yourself against this dangerous confusion?

Being Pleased with the Decree While Rejecting Evil
Imam al-Nasafi explains the believer's attitude toward decree and evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a believer can be pleased with Allah's decree regarding evil without being pleased with evil as a personal attribute or action.

Core Idea
Pleased with the decree, not the evil decreed.

Imam al-Nasafi now applies the qada'/maqdi distinction to the believer's attitude. He explains that one can and should be pleased with Allah's decree that creates evil as evil, while simultaneously hating that evil for oneself. This resolves an apparent tension in loving Allah's will while shunning sin.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the three scenarios presented.
The scenarios involve pleasure with the decree, pleasure with the evil attribute, and pleasure with the decree but not the attribute.
2
Map the scenarios
Identify each case: 1) pleased that Allah makes kufr evil, 2) not pleased, 3) pleased with the decree but not with kufr as a personal attribute.
3
Reflect on wisdom
Understand why the third scenario is the correct stance: it balances love for Allah's wisdom with personal aversion to sin.
This shows how divine decree and human responsibility coexist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?
When you see another Muslim fall into sin, do you blame Allah's decree or do you recognize that sin stems from the person's own acquisition? How does this passage change the way you view sinners—with compassion or condemnation? Commit today to hate the sin but not the sinner, and to trust the decree behind it.

Refutation of al-Ka'bi's Hadith Argument
Imam al-Nasafi refutes al-Ka'bi's hadith argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Nasafi's refutation of al-Ka'bi's use of the hadith 'Whoever is not pleased with My decree...' and how the hadith applies to trials, not to chosen sins.

Core Idea
The hadith refers to trials, not to decreed sins.

Al-Ka'bi, a Mu'tazilite, invoked a well-known hadith to argue against the Ash'ari view that disbelief is decreed by Allah. Imam al-Nasafi responds by pointing out that the hadith concerns patience with calamities, not with eternal punishment or chosen sins. He also turns the argument against al-Ka'bi's own sect, showing their inconsistency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note al-Ka'bi's hadith and the response.
2
Identify the two parts of the response
First, the hadith is about illnesses and calamities. Second, the Mu'tazila themselves believe eternal punishment is decreed by Allah, so they should be pleased with it.
3
Understand the final point
The author explains that disbelievers are pleased with their disbelief (they cling to it), so the hadith about displeasure does not apply to them. It applies to those distressed by trials.
This shows the hadith does not conflict with the decree of disbelief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقضاء الله تعالى حق وصواب وبه احتج الكعبي. وعندنا أن الكفر مقضي الله تعالى لا قضاؤه، وقضاؤه حق وصواب، ومقضيه باطل، وقضاء هذا المقضي صواب، لما فيه من الحكمة على ما بينا في مسألة خلق الأفعال، فمن رضي يجعل الله تعالى الكفر باطلاً قبيحًا شرًا فقد رضى بقضاء الله تعالى، ومن لم يرض بذلك فهو غير راض بقضاء الله، ومن رضي بذلك ولم يرض أن يكون الكفر صفة له ولم يحب أن يفعله في نفسه فقد رضي بقضاء الله تعالى ولم يرضى بما يوجب مقته وتعذيبه. واحتج الكعبي بقول النبي عليه السلام: من لم يرض بقضائي ولم يصبر على بلائي فليطلب ربا سواي. وقد بينا أنا رضينا بقضاء الله تعالى وأعلمنا كيفيته على أن حقيقة الخبر في الأمراض والمصائب ألا ترى أن الخلود في النار من قضائه عند المعتزلة إذ لا يثبتون الله تعالى تخليدًا سوى معنى الخلود، فليرض به الكعبي لنفسه، وإلا فليطلب ربا سواه. يحققه أن الكفر عندنا وإن كان بقضاء الله تعالى إلا أن من قضى عليه ذلك یرضی به ويتمسك به تمسكًا لا يرضى بالزوال عنه، وإنما الذي لا يرضى به المقضي عليه ويضجر منه في الأحايين هو الأمراض والمصائب، والمعتزلة لا يرضون بها إلا بعوض فليطلبوا ربًّا سوى من قضى بها عليهم.
And the decree (qada') of Allah Most High is true and correct, and with it al-Ka'bi argued. And according to us, disbelief (kufr) is the decreed thing (maqdi) of Allah Most High, not His decree (qada'), and His decree is true and correct, and its decreed thing is false, and the decree of this decreed thing is correct, due to the wisdom in it, as we explained in the issue of the creation of acts. So whoever is pleased that Allah Most High makes disbelief false, ugly, and evil has indeed been pleased with the decree of Allah Most High, and whoever is not pleased with that is not pleased with the decree of Allah. And whoever is pleased with that but is not pleased that disbelief be an attribute for him and does not love to perform it in himself has been pleased with the decree of Allah Most High and has not been pleased with what necessitates His wrath and punishment. And al-Ka'bi argued with the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him: 'Whoever is not pleased with My decree and does not have patience with My trial, let him seek a lord other than Me.' And we have explained that we are pleased with the decree of Allah Most High and we know its manner, such that the true meaning of the news concerns illnesses and calamities. Do you not see that eternal punishment in the Fire is from His decree according to the Mu'tazila, since they do not affirm for Allah Most High causing to remain eternal except the meaning of remaining eternal. So let al-Ka'bi be pleased with it for himself, otherwise let him seek a lord other than Him. He verifies that disbelief, according to us, even though it is by the decree of Allah Most High, yet the one upon whom that is decreed is pleased with it and clings to it, not being pleased with its removal. Rather, that which the one decreed upon is not pleased with and is distressed by at times is illnesses and calamities, and the Mu'tazila are not pleased with them except with compensation. So let them seek a lord other than the One who decreed them upon them.
Source pages p. 987

Commentary
This passage defines the distinction between qada' (divine decree) and maqdi (the decreed thing). The author begins by stating that Allah's qada' is always true and correct, but the maqdi—such as disbelief—may be false and evil. He clarifies that being pleased with Allah's decree does not require being pleased with disbelief as a personal attribute or action; one can be pleased with the decree for its wisdom while hating the evil of the decreed. The author then addresses the Mu'tazilite al-Ka'bi, who used the hadith 'Whoever is not pleased with My decree...' to argue against the Ash'ari position. The author responds by affirming that they are indeed pleased with Allah's decree, but the hadith refers to patience with trials (illnesses and calamities), not with eternal punishment in Hell. He points out that even al-Ka'bi's own sect (Mu'tazila) holds that the eternal punishment in Hell is from Allah's decree, so al-Ka'bi himself should be pleased with that decree, otherwise he should seek another lord. The author then explains that disbelief, though decreed, is embraced by the disbeliever willingly, not out of compulsion—he is pleased with it and does not want to abandon it. In contrast, illnesses and calamities are disliked by those afflicted, and the Mu'tazila only accept them with compensation. Thus, the hadith about patience applies to trials, not to chosen sins.
