The Apostate Example and Wisdom Conclusion
Nasafi presents the second example and concludes with the wisdom argument and a Quranic proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the example of the apostate further refutes the Mu'tazili doctrine, and how Nasafi concludes that divine actions are wise even if their wisdom is hidden, and that calling them foolish is disbelief.

Core Idea
God's wisdom in allowing apostasy proves His actions are wise, not foolish.

Nasafi continues his refutation with a second example: a Muslim who later apostatizes. He argues that it would have been more beneficial for him to die before apostasy, yet God keeps him alive, knowing the outcome. This again shows that God does not always do what is most beneficial for the individual. Nasafi then draws a sharp conclusion: to claim that such divine actions are foolish (safah) is itself disbelief. The Mu'tazila's error stems from their ignorance of the true nature of divine wisdom. Finally, he reinforces the argument with a Quranic verse that shows God grants respite so that sinners increase in sin.

Guided Path
1
Read the apostate example
Read the passage from 'وكذا من عاش مدة' to the end. Notice the parallel structure with the previous example.
The structure is similar: hypothetical better outcome vs. actual divine action.
2
Understand the wisdom argument
Nasafi says 'دل أن ذلك كان حكمة' (it indicates that was wisdom). He emphasizes that ignorance of wisdom does not negate wisdom.
3
Note the strong warning against calling God foolish
Nasafi states that calling God's action 'safah' (foolishness) is kufr. This is a serious theological boundary.
4
Read the concluding verse
The verse Āl ʿImrān 3:178 is cited to show that God's respite is purposeful, not meaningless.
The verse: 'We only grant them respite so that they may increase in sin.'

Misconception & Correction

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is the ruling on someone who claims that God's action of keeping the apostate alive is foolishness (safah)?
A) It is a valid opinion.
B) It is a sin but not disbelief.
C) It is disbelief (kufr).
D) It is a sign of deep thought.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial or see someone else suffer, do you truly believe that God's wisdom is at work even if you cannot see the benefit? What specific thought or action in your life reveals that you doubt this truth?
?
If God allowed you to persist in a sin for years before you repented, do you recognize that His wisdom is behind that delay? How does that change the way you view your past mistakes?
?
The Mu'tazila rejected this wisdom because it seemed foolish to them. Have you ever rejected a command of God or a divine decree in your heart because you thought it made no sense? Will you commit today to trusting God's wisdom over your own understanding?

The Refutation of the Al-Aslah Principle
Al-Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazili principle of al-aslah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazili principle of al-aslah by showing that human reason cannot determine what is truly beneficial in divine actions, and that the Mu'tazili position leads to a contradiction.

Core Idea
Human intellect cannot impose what is beneficial on God.

In this passage, al-Nasafi directly confronts the Mu'tazili claim that God must always do what is most beneficial for His servants. He argues that this claim is not only false but leads to absurd consequences when applied consistently. By following the exchange, you will see how the Maturidi school defends God's absolute wisdom and freedom.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazili claim
Begin by reading the Mu'tazili argument as presented by al-Nasafi: they claim that God only does what is most beneficial, even if we do not understand it.
Notice how they try to salvage their position after previous refutations.
2
Follow the reductio ad absurdum
Al-Nasafi turns their logic against them by proposing that creating disbelief could be most beneficial. Examine how he parallels their own reasoning about divine foreknowledge.
Pay attention to the symmetry he creates between the two cases.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Al-Nasafi concludes that human intellect cannot fully grasp benefit and harm in divine actions. Consider why this means we should not impose obligations or restrictions on God.
This underscores the Maturidi emphasis on God's wisdom and freedom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلما تحيروا في هذا زعم بعضهم: ثبت لنا أن الله تعالى لا يفعل إلا ما هو الأصلح فيكون جميع ما يفعل أصلح، وإن كنا لا نعقل نحن وجه المصلحة. فيقال لهم: بل يعقل فيه كل عاقل وجه المفسدة والمضرة، وعرف أن ما قلتم باطل. ثم نقول لهم ما أنكرتم على من يقول لكم إذا ثبت بما بينا أنه تعالى حكيم علم أن منع المصلحة والأصلح حكمة وإن كنا لا نعقل وجه الحكمة، ويقال لهم ما أنكرتم على هذا أن يكون تخليق الكفر أصلح للعبد؟ فإن قالوا: كيف يكون ذلك أصلح وقد يتضرر به العبد؟ قلنا: وكيف يكون الإبقاء إلى أن يكفر أو يرتد أصلح له وهو يتضرر به، وإن قالوا: لا يتصور من العبد الإيمان إذا خلق فيه الكفر . قلنا ولا يتصور منه الإيمان إذا علم أنه يكفر فإن كان هذا مصلحة فذاك أيضا مصلحة، وإن لم يكن ذاك مصلحة فهذا أيضا ليس بمصلحة وإن عقلا هذا مبلغ معرفته بالمصلحة والمفسدة لحقيق أن لا يعرف صاحبه به الحكمة والسفه ولحري أن لا يتحكم على الله بالإيجاب عليه تارة والحجر عليه أخرى، والله الموفق.
When they became perplexed about this, some of them claimed: It is established for us that God Most High does not do anything except what is most beneficial, so all that He does is most beneficial, even if we ourselves do not understand the aspect of benefit. Then it is said to them: Rather, every intelligent person understands in it the aspect of harm and injury, and knows that what you say is false. Then we say to them: What do you deny from one who says to you: Since it is established by what we have shown that He Most High is wise—knowledge that withholding benefit and the most beneficial is wisdom, even if we do not understand the aspect of wisdom? And it is said to them: What do you deny from this, that the creation of disbelief could be most beneficial for the servant? For if they say: How can that be most beneficial when the servant is harmed by it? We say: And how can letting him remain until he disbelieves or apostatizes be most beneficial for him when he is harmed by it? And if they say: It is not possible for the servant to have faith if disbelief is created in him. We say: Nor is it possible for him to have faith if He knows that he will disbelieve. For if this is a benefit, then that is also a benefit; and if that is not a benefit, then this is also not a benefit. And if the intellect has reached this limit of its knowledge of benefit and harm, it is worthy that its possessor does not recognize wisdom and foolishness, and it is more worthy that he does not arrogantly presume to impose obligations on God at one time and to restrict Him at another. And God is the Granter of success.
Source pages p. 1002

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the refutation against the Mu'tazili doctrine of al-aslah (the most beneficial). Some Mu'tazila, perplexed by the previous argument, claim that God does only what is most beneficial, even if we do not understand the benefit. Al-Nasafi responds that every intelligent person can see the harm in the examples given, so their claim is false. He then uses a reductio ad absurdum: If God's withholding benefit can be wisdom, then why cannot creating disbelief (takhliq al-kufr) in a servant be for his own best interest? When the Mu'tazila object that the servant is harmed by disbelief, al-Nasafi retorts that allowing the servant to live until he disbelieves is also harmful. And if they argue that faith is impossible if disbelief is created in him, al-Nasafi points out that by their own doctrine—since God knows he will disbelieve—faith is also impossible. Thus the Mu'tazili position is symmetrical and collapses. He concludes that human intellect cannot fully grasp benefit and harm in divine actions, so one should not arrogantly impose obligations or restrictions on God.

Key Terms
الأصلح
تخليق الكفر
الحكمة
المصلحة
المفسدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila believe that humans can rationally determine what actions are beneficial for God to do.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that human reason is limited; one cannot know the true benefit of divine actions, so we should not impose obligations on God.

Checkpoint
What is the Mu'tazili objection to the idea that creating kufr could be in the servant's best interest?
A) It would make God unjust.
B) It is impossible because the servant would be harmed.
C) They say: How can that be most beneficial when the servant is harmed by it?
D) They have no objection.

Key Takeaway
God's wisdom is absolute; human reason cannot judge His actions as beneficial or harmful.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a tragedy or hardship in your life, do you trust that God's wisdom is absolute, or do you secretly believe you could suggest a better plan? Which specific trial are you currently questioning God about? Commit today to recite 'I believe in the decree, its good and evil, and that my intellect is incapable of fully grasping His wisdom.'
?
The Mu'tazila tried to impose what they thought was best for others onto God. In your dealings with family, friends, or even in your own life, when do you find yourself acting as if you know what is truly beneficial for them, overriding God's decree? This week, in one concrete situation, choose to remain silent and trust God's plan instead of insisting on your own solution.
?
If God's creation of disbelief in someone could be wise even if we don't see it, then what right do you have to judge anyone's fate or to despair of God's mercy for yourself? Think of one person you have written off as hopeless or one sin you think God cannot forgive. In light of this lesson, surrender your judgment to God and ask Him to give you hope.

Refuting the Claim that God Must Create the Best
A dialectical refutation of the Mu'tazila claim that God must create the best.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi refutation of the Mu'tazila claim that God must create what is best for the servant, and why human reason cannot impose obligations on God.

Core Idea
God's wisdom is not bound by human perceptions of benefit and harm.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazila argument that God is obliged to create the best for each servant. The author uses a series of logical reversals to show that the Mu'tazila position is inconsistent and that human intellect cannot fully grasp divine wisdom. As we read, we'll follow the back-and-forth dialectic and see how the author turns their own reasoning against them.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the full English translation. Let the author's own rhythm and structure come through before interpreting.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila claim
The Mu'tazila claim that God must create what is best (al-aṣlaḥ) for the servant. Notice how the author first challenges this by pointing out that even rational people perceive harm in some acts.
3
Trace the dialectic
Follow the back-and-forth: the author raises an example (creating disbelief), the Mu'tazila object, the author responds by showing inconsistency. Pay attention to the comparison with keeping the servant alive until disbelief.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that human intellect cannot fully judge benefit and harm in God's actions. Consider why this is a powerful rebuttal against imposing obligations on God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجه المصلحة . فيقال لهم: بل يعقل فيه كل عاقل وجه المفسدة والمضرة، وعرف أن ما قلتم باطل.
ثم نقول لهم ما أنكرتم على من يقول لكم إذا ثبت بما بينا أنه تعالى يمنع أنه تعالى حكيم علم أن منع المصلحة والأصلح حكمة وإن كنا لا نعقل وجه الحكمة، ويقال لهم ما أنكرتم على هذا أن يكون تخليق الكفر أصلح للعبد؟ فإن قالوا: كيف يكون ذلك أصلح وقد يتضرر به العبد؟ قلنا: وكيف يكون الإبقاء إلى أن يكفر أو يرتد أصلح له وهو يتضرر به، وإن قالوا: لا يتصور من العبد الإيمان إذا خلق فيه الكفر . قلنا ولا يتصور منه الإيمان إذا علم أنه يكفر فإن كان هذا مصلحة فذاك أيضا مصلحة، وإن لم يكن ذاك مصلحة فهذا أيضا ليس بمصلحة وإن عقلا هذا مبلغ معرفته بالمصلحة والمفسدة لحقيق أن لا يعرف صاحبه به الحكمة والسفه ولحري أن لا يتحكم على الله بالإيجاب عليه تارة والحجر عليه أخرى، والله الموفق.
The aspect of benefit. So it is said to them: Rather every rational person perceives in it the aspect of harm and injury, and they know that what you said is false. Then we say to them: What do you deny from one who says to you: When it is established through what we have explained that He, exalted, prevents [the best]—that He, exalted, is wise, [and] He knows that preventing benefit and the best is wisdom even if we do not perceive the aspect of wisdom? And it is said to them: What do you deny from this—that creating disbelief is best for the servant? For if they say: How can that be best when the servant is harmed by it? We say: And how is keeping [him alive] until he disbelieves or apostatizes best for him while he is harmed by it? And if they say: Faith from the servant is inconceivable when disbelief is created in him. We say: Nor is faith conceivable from him when it is known that he will disbelieve. So if this is a benefit, then that also is a benefit; and if that is not a benefit, then this also is not a benefit. And if the intellect—this is the extent of its knowledge of benefit and harm—it is proper that its possessor should not know wisdom and foolishness, and it is fitting that he should not presume to impose obligations on God or restrict Him. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 1003

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila claim that God must always create what is best (al-aṣlaḥ) for the servant. The author first points out that even the Mu'tazila admit that in some acts there is both benefit and harm, so their claim that God must avoid harm is inconsistent. He then turns their logic against them: if they say that creating disbelief cannot be best because it harms the servant, then why do they allow God to keep the servant alive until he disbelieves, which also harms him? They also claim that faith is impossible if disbelief is created in the servant, but the author retorts that faith is also impossible if God knows the servant will disbelieve. Thus, if one case is a benefit, the other is likewise; and if one is not, the other also is not. The author concludes that human intellect is too limited to judge what is truly beneficial or harmful for God, and that it is wrong to impose obligations on God or restrict Him.

Key Terms
مصلحة (benefit)
أصلح (best)
مفسدة (harm)
حكمة (wisdom)
تخليق الكفر (creating disbelief)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God is obligated to do what appears best to human reason.
Correction: The author shows that human reason cannot fully determine what is truly beneficial or harmful in God's actions, and that God's wisdom transcends our limited comprehension.

Checkpoint
What inconsistency does the author highlight in the Mu'tazila position?
A) They accept that God keeps the servant alive until disbelief (which harms him) but reject creating disbelief as harmful.
B) They claim God is wise but deny His wisdom.
C) They say God must create the best, but also say He creates evil.
D) They argue from reason but reject revelation.

Key Takeaway
Human intellect cannot fully judge benefit and harm in God's actions, so we cannot impose that God must create what we deem best.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When has your own perception of what is 'best' for you clashed with what actually happened, and how did you react? Do you still trust that God's wisdom surpasses your understanding, even when the outcome seems harmful?
?
The author argues that human intellect cannot fully judge benefit and harm. Think of a time you were certain something was harmful, only later to realize it was beneficial. If you truly believe God's wisdom is unlimited, what would change in how you respond to trials you don't understand?
?
This passage rebukes those who impose on God what He 'must' do. Search your heart: do you ever subconsciously expect God to act according to your sense of fairness or logic? What would it cost you to surrender that expectation entirely?

Refuting the View That God's Actions Include Injustice and Misguidance
The author refutes the notion that God's actions can be unjust or misleading.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributing injustice or misguidance to God is logically and theologically incoherent, based on the principle that all divine actions are beneficial and gracious.

Core Idea
Asking God to remove His blessings is asking for harm.

This passage tackles a serious misunderstanding about God's actions. Some have wrongly thought that God's decrees can include injustice or misguidance. Using a logical analysis of the 'states' (ahwal) that come from God, the author demonstrates that such a view is self-contradictory and akin to mockery. Let's unpack his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without over-analyzing, then the literal translation. Let the author's argument flow naturally before you interpret.
Notice the strong opening: 'yusaffihu wa yajūru wa yazlimu'.
2
Identify key terms and logical structure
Identify the terms 'ahwal', 'maslaha', 'lutf' and track how the author builds from a premise (all states are benefit) to a conclusion (asking to remove them is asking for harm).
The argument is conditional: 'if all states from God are benefit, then their opposites are harm'.
3
Trace the reductio ad absurdum
Follow the author's reasoning: if someone asks God to remove beneficial states, they are effectively asking for harmful opposites. This would be ignorant and even mockery.
Compare this to the disbelievers who mockingly asked for punishment.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
Internalize that the passage guards against the misconception that God's actions can be unjust. All divine actions are good; evil is only apparent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يسفه ويجور ويظلم، ومن هذا ظنه مما علم الله تعالى عباده ليدعوه به واشتغل به الرسل والأنبياء والأولياء والصالحون، فتجديد الإسلام له أولى، وبالله العصمة. يحققه أن ما عندهم من الأحوال لما كانت مصلحة ولطفا عندهم جميع فيكون ما يضادها من الأحوال أضداد لها، ولما كانت تلك الأحوال الكائنة عندهم لطفا ومصلحة كانت رحمة وهداية وإرشادا وتوفيقا ومعونة ونصرة، فيكون أضدادها سخطة وإضلالاً وإغواء وخذلانا ولعنا وإبعادا وإفسادًا فيصير سائل كشف تلك الأحوال وإزالتها وإبدالها بما يضادها سائلاً من الله تعالى أن يزيل عنه المصلحة واللطف والرحمة والهداية والتوفيق والمعونة والنصرة، ويبدله مكانها أضدادها التي ذكرناها، ومن حمل أمر الله تعالى عباده بالدعاء وفعل الأنبياء والأولياء على هذا فلا غاية لجهله، ثم معلوم أن مظهر الرغبة في إضلال الله تعالى وخذلانه وإغوائه وإفساده مستخف به غير عارف به بل مثله في الخلق استهانة واستهزاء، وعلى مثله كان استهزاء الكفرة برسول الله عليه السلام بقولهم انتنَا بِعَذَابِ اللهِ ونحوه، وذلك مما لا يحتمله قلب من أقر به، وبالله العصمة.
He declares foolish, unjust, and oppressive. And from this is his assumption of what God Almighty taught His servants so that they would call upon Him and the messengers, prophets, saints, and righteous ones occupied themselves with it. So renewing Islam is more appropriate for him, and with God is protection. He verifies that the states that they have, since they were benefit and grace for them all, so what opposes them from the states are opposites to them. And since those states occurring with them were grace and benefit, they were mercy, guidance, direction, success, aid, and victory. So their opposites become wrath, misguidance, seduction, abandonment, curse, distance, and corruption. So the asker of the removal of those states and their replacement with what opposes them becomes a supplicant to God Almighty to remove from him benefit, grace, mercy, guidance, success, aid, and victory, and replace for him in their place their opposites that we mentioned. And whoever interprets God Almighty's command to His servants of prayer and the action of the prophets and saints upon this, there is no limit to his ignorance. Then it is known that the one who shows desire for God's misguidance, abandonment, seduction, and corruption is belittling Him, not knowing Him, rather his example among creation is disdain and mockery. And upon his example was the mockery of the disbelievers towards the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, by their saying 'Bring us the punishment of God' and the like. And that is something that the heart of one who acknowledges Him cannot bear. And with God is protection.
Source pages p. 1007

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a view that attributes injustice and misguidance to God. The author first declares that anyone who holds such a view is foolish, unjust, and oppressive. He then explains that the states (ahwal) that come from God are all benefit and grace, and their opposites are the opposites of mercy. Therefore, asking God to remove these states is asking for harm. The author emphasizes that the prophets and saints engaged in prayer, but not in the way of asking for harm. Finally, he compares such a request to the mockery of the disbelievers. The passage asserts that God's actions are entirely good and that ascription of evil to Him is ignorance.

لطف (lutf)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God can decree injustice or misguidance for people as a punishment or for other reasons.
Correction: The author argues that all divine actions are benefit and grace; their opposites are harm and misguidance. Therefore, attributing injustice to God is a misunderstanding of His attributes and leads to absurd consequences.

A) They are sometimes benefit and sometimes harm.

Key Takeaway
God's actions are entirely beneficial and gracious; what appears harmful is only because we misunderstand the greater good.

?
If God's guidance is the greatest blessing, are you truly grateful for it? Or do you sometimes take it for granted or even wish for a life without its responsibilities? Pause and ask God to increase your gratitude, and then recite a du'a of thanks from your heart.

The Mu'tazilite Doctrine of Aslah Leads to Contradiction
Author argues Mu'tazilite aslah principle implies God lacks power to reform disbelievers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite view that God must give the most beneficial (aslah) to every person leads to the conclusion that God is not able to bring about faith in disbelievers.

Core Idea
If giving the maximum benefit fails, God cannot be all-powerful according to Mu'tazilite logic.

The Mu'tazilites held that God is obligated to give every person the most beneficial option (aslah) to facilitate belief. But what happens when a disbeliever, having received the utmost benefit, still rejects faith? In this passage, the author exposes a devastating contradiction: if God has given all He can, yet the disbeliever remains defiant, then God cannot truly possess the power to reform whomever He wills. This argument strikes at the heart of the Mu'tazilite conception of divine justice and power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Note that the passage ends mid-sentence; the argument is still complete enough.
2
Identify the logical steps
The author presents a three-step argument: (1) God gives utmost aslah to each disbeliever, (2) the disbeliever does not believe, (3) therefore God lacks power to reform. Trace these steps in the text.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: starting from Mu'tazilite premises and leading to an unacceptable conclusion.
3
Reflect on the contradiction
Ask yourself: why would the Mu'tazilites find this conclusion problematic? What does it imply about their view of God's power and obligation?
The Mu'tazilites believed God must do what is most beneficial, but this argument shows that if He does, He still fails, so He is not omnipotent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لا شك أن كل كافر قد أعطاه الله تعالى عندهم غاية ما في مقدوره من الأصلح ولم يؤمن به الكافر فلم يكن على رأيهم الله تعالى ملك ما به الصلاح ولا قدرة على إصلاح من يشاء من عباده أن يصلح لو بذل جميع ما في خزائنه إذ ما من شيء يملك به الصلاح أو يقدر عليه إلا ويكون به على ذلك القدر فساده، ثم لا
Then there is no doubt that every disbeliever, God Almighty has given him, according to them, the utmost of what is within His capability of the most beneficial, and the disbeliever did not believe. So, according to their view, God Almighty does not possess what gives well-being nor power to reform whomever He wills of His servants to reform, even if He expended all that is in His treasuries, since there is nothing by which He possesses well-being or has power over it except that by that amount there is corruption of it. Then no.
Source pages p. 1008

Commentary
This passage presents an argument against the Mu'tazilite view that God must give the most beneficial (aslah) to every person. The author says that every disbeliever has been given the utmost of what God can give of the most beneficial, yet the disbeliever does not believe. According to the Mu'tazilite view, this implies that God does not possess the means to give well-being (salah) or the power to reform whomever He wills, because if He could, He would have succeeded. The argument highlights a contradiction in the Mu'tazilite position: if God gives the maximum benefit and still fails, then He is not able to bring about faith. The passage ends incomplete.

Key Terms
كافر (kafir)
أصلح (aslah)
صلاح (salah)
قدرة (qudra)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mu'tazilites believe that God must give the most beneficial to every person, and if He does, all should believe.
Correction: The passage shows that even if God gives the utmost benefit, disbelief can persist, which undermines the Mu'tazilite view that God's obligation guarantees faith. It also implies that God's power is limited if He cannot overcome disbelief after maximum effort.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude about the Mu'tazilite view that God must give the most beneficial?
A) It shows God's mercy.
B) It leads to the conclusion that God cannot reform those He wills.
C) It proves that disbelievers are not given enough.
D) It is correct.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite obligation of aslah leads to the absurd conclusion that God cannot reform those He wills.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

In light of this lesson, how does the Mu'tazilite error of imposing obligations on God mirror your own heart's expectations? What would it mean for you to truly accept that God does not owe you anything?
?
The reductio argument shows that if God had done everything to make a disbeliever believe, but they still did not, it would imply God is powerless. Do you truly believe that your own faith is entirely a gift from God, or do you secretly think you contributed to it? How can you cultivate genuine humility before God's absolute power over your faith?
?
This passage guards against the assumption that God must act according to human notions of 'most beneficial.' Where in your life have you demanded that God work in a way that makes sense to you? Are you willing to surrender your own definitions of what is best for you and trust that His decree, even when it seems not beneficial, is the true ṣalāḥ?

The Universal Benefit of Supplication
Nasafi refutes an objection about the benefit of supplication for the already righteous.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the command to supplicate remains beneficial even for those who already avoid sin, according to Nasafi's argument.

Core Idea
Supplication is always good, whether it changes behavior or not.

Nasafi anticipates a potential objection: if supplication causes people to avoid sin, what about those who already do so? Wouldn't the command be pointless for them? In this passage, he provides a concise refutation that upholds the wisdom of the universal command to supplicate.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to see the structure. Then read the translation to understand the core argument.
The objection is presented as a statement from an unnamed opponent.
2
Identify the premise of the objection
What is the objector's assumption? They assume that the benefit of supplication is only to help one avoid sin, so if one already does so, supplication is unnecessary.
3
Analyze the refutation's logic
Nasafi's reply has two parts: (1) the command is universal for all legally responsible persons, (2) the act of supplication is inherently good. He then argues that wisdom does not require benefit for every individual; many do not receive the subtle benefit, so the command remains valid for the majority.
Note the phrase 'يجب خروجه عن الحكمة' – it would necessitate departure from wisdom.
4
Understand the conclusion
The objector is silenced (فانقطع) and resorts to an invalid objection. This shows the strength of Nasafi's argument.
5
Reflect on the broader implication
Consider how this argument upholds the wisdom of divine commands even when individual outcomes vary.
Connects to the theme of divine wisdom (الحكمة).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاعترض عليه فقال : إذا كان فيمن يدعو من يترك المعصية فقد حصلت له اللطيفة وصحت الفائدة في الدعاء. قيل له : الأمر بالدعاء عام في المكلفين وكانت المسألة حسنة من كل واحد من السائلين ويجب خروجه عن الحكمة فيمن لم يكن في مقدوره له لطيفة وذلك أكثر في العدد ممن حصلت له الفائدة. فانقطع وانتقل إلى ما لا يصح من الاعتراض.
He objected to him and said: If among those who supplicate is one who refrains from sin, then the subtle benefit has been obtained for him and the benefit of the supplication is realized. It was said to him: The command to supplicate is general for all legally responsible persons, and the request is good from each supplicant, and it would necessitate departure from wisdom regarding one for whom a subtle benefit is not within his capacity, and such persons are more numerous than those who obtain the benefit. So he was silenced and turned to an invalid objection.
Source pages p. 1008

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its refutation concerning the divine wisdom behind commanding supplication. The objector argues that if a person is already inclined to avoid sin, then the supplication's purpose is already fulfilled, making the command superfluous. The refutation states that the command to supplicate is universal for all morally responsible individuals, and the act of asking is inherently good from each person. However, wisdom requires that for those who cannot receive the subtle benefit from supplication (e.g., because they are already righteous), the benefit is not intended; rather, such individuals are numerous, and the command remains valid for the majority who do benefit. The objector is thus silenced and resorts to an invalid objection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Supplication only benefits those who are sinful.
Correction: Supplication is an act of worship beneficial for all, even the righteous, because it is good in itself and the command is universal.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that the command to supplicate is not undermined even for those who already avoid sin?
A) Because such people are few and the command is for the majority.
B) Because the supplication itself is a good act for everyone, and the benefit is not the only purpose.
C) Because the objector's premise is false.
D) Because the subtle benefit is only obtained through supplication.

Key Takeaway
The command to supplicate is universal and beneficial even if some do not receive its apparent benefit, because the act itself is good.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

How does the statement 'the request is good from each supplicant' challenge that doubt? Write down one specific area of your life where you will persist in dua despite not seeing results.
?
The objector in this passage thought that the benefit of dua depends on one's state. Is there a hidden assumption in your heart that your worship is only valuable if you feel a certain way? What will you do this week to act on the truth that the act itself is good, regardless of your feelings?

How Supplication Transforms Benefit and Harm
Nasafi answers an objection about praying for healing when illness might be beneficial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi defends the command to pray for healing when illness may be a benefit, by showing that supplication changes the moral status of the situation.

Core Idea
Supplication transforms a benefit into a harm, making its removal a new benefit.

This passage addresses a subtle objection: if illness can be a divine benefit, why would Allah command us to pray for its removal? Nasafi's answer hinges on the idea that the act of supplication itself changes what is beneficial and harmful. Let's read his response carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: an objection followed by 'we say' (قلنا).
2
Understand the objector's dilemma
The objector presents a dilemma: if illness is not a benefit, praying for removal is unnecessary; if it is a benefit, why ask to remove it? Identify this dilemma clearly.
3
Grasp the key move: supplication changes the moral valence
Nasafi's answer: at the time of supplication, what was a benefit becomes a harm, so removal becomes beneficial. See how he reframes the situation.
This resolves the apparent contradiction by showing that the command to pray itself alters the context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أن هذا الاعتراض لا يستقيم في حق الدعاء ومسألة كشف ما به من المرض، فإن ذلك إن لم يكن مصلحة قد فعل ما ليس بمصلحة وإن كان مصلحة فلم أمر بمسألة دفع المصلحة؟ فإن قلت إنه عند الدعاء يصير مفسدة والمصلحة حينئذ تصير في دفعه وإثبات ضده الذي كان حتى الآن مفسدة. قلنا: لما أمر بالدعاء وفيه حصل ما هو من المصلحة مفسدة وجعل ما ليس فيه من المفسدة مصلحة ولو جعل كذلك أي منفعة لهذا السائل في ذلك الوقت وقد كان قبله في مصلحة والمفسدة عنه منتفية، والله الموفق.
Moreover, this objection does not apply to supplication and the matter of removing the illness upon him. For if it [the illness] is not a benefit, then he has done that which is not a benefit; and if it is a benefit, then why was he commanded to ask for the removal of the benefit? If you say that at the time of supplication it becomes a harm, and the benefit then lies in its removal and the establishment of its opposite which was until now a harm, we say: Since He commanded supplication, and in it there occurs that which was a benefit becomes a harm, and He makes that which had no harm in it a benefit; and if He made it so, what benefit is there for this supplicant at that time, while before he was in a benefit and harm was removed from him? And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 1008

Commentary
This passage refines the objection concerning supplication for the removal of illness. The objector argues: if the illness is not a benefit (maslaha), then praying to remove it is neutral; but if the illness is a benefit, then praying to remove it would be asking to discard a benefit, which seems contradictory. The response explains that supplication itself changes the situation: what was a benefit before supplication becomes a harm at the time of supplication, and its removal becomes the new benefit. Thus, the command to pray is rational. The author concludes that the supplicant's state changes: before prayer he was in benefit (health) with no harm; after prayer, the benefit is in the removal of the now-harmful illness. God's wisdom is consistent.

Key Terms
الدعاء
المصلحة
المفسدة
كشف المرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The benefit or harm of a situation is fixed and unchanging.
Correction: Nasafi shows that benefit and harm can change based on Divine command and the act of supplication.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is it not contradictory for God to command someone to pray for the removal of an illness that was originally a benefit?
A) Because the illness was never a benefit.
B) Because the illness becomes a harm at the time of supplication, so removing it is beneficial.
C) Because God's commands are not subject to logic.
D) Because the benefit is only in the supplication itself, not in the outcome.

Key Takeaway
Supplication, commanded by Allah, redefines what is beneficial, thus resolving the apparent contradiction of praying to remove a benefit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your supplication can change the reality of what is good and bad in your life? Or do you pray with a hidden doubt that the situation is fixed?
?
What would it mean for you to actually live as if your dua can transform harms into benefits? Will you commit to praying with that conviction starting today?
?
When you face a trial that seems 'beneficial' in theory but is painful in practice, do you dare to pray for its removal, trusting that Allah can make that removal a new benefit? Or do you silently accept the pain, thinking it must remain a 'benefit'?

Two-fold Refutation of Mu'tazilite al-Aslah
Nasafi argues that God gave disbelievers all possible benefit, and two consequences follow.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's two-fold argument showing that God cannot be obligated to provide the most beneficial (al-aslah) to disbelievers, and how this refutes the Mu'tazilite position.

Core Idea
God's giving more to a disbeliever only increases corruption, not benefit.

The Mu'tazila held that God must always do what is most beneficial (al-aslah) for His servants. Nasafi here argues that for disbelievers, God has already given the maximum possible benefit within His power, yet they disbelieve. Therefore, giving more would only worsen their disobedience and punishment. This leads to two consequences that expose the inconsistency of the Mu'tazilite doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the logical flow: premise about what God has given, then the conclusion that giving more is corruptive.
Pay attention to the two 'aspects' at the end.
2
Understand the premise
The author assumes the Mu'tazila believe God gives every disbeliever the utmost possible benefit. Yet the disbeliever does not believe. This sets up the problem.
3
Follow the first aspect
The first aspect: Giving to one known not to believe is more corruptive for him, not beneficial. So God's act is corruptive, not most beneficial.
This uses the principle that more blessings with rejection makes sin worse.
4
Follow the second aspect
The second aspect: Since only corruptive acts are within God's power for such persons, the most beneficial is not within His power. Thus the Mu'tazila demand what God cannot do.
This contradicts their own principle that God does not charge beyond capacity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لا شك أن كل كافر قد أعطاه الله تعالى عندهم غاية ما في مقدوره من الأصلح ولم يؤمن به الكافر فلم يكن على رأيهم الله تعالى ملك ما به الصلاح ولا قدرة على إصلاح من يشاء من عباده أن يصلح لو بذل جميع ما في خزائنه إذ ما من شيء يملك به الصلاح أو يقدر عليه إلا ويكون به على ذلك القدر فساده، ثم لا يصلح أحد به فكيف يحتمل إلزام ما لا يملكه ولا يقوى عليه إذ ما يملكه هو لا يحصل به الصلاح، وما لا يحصل به الصلاح يكون أصلح من غيره لاستوائهما في أن لا أثر لهما في تحصيل الصلاح بل إعطائه يكون أفسد ؛ إذ كفره وعصيانه معه أقبح والعقوبة عليه أغلظ؛ إذ العصيان مع توفر الزواجر وترك الطاعة مع كثرة النعم والدواعي أقبح واستيجاب العقوبة عليه أقوى. فبعد ذلك تقرر هذا الكلام من وجهين أحدهما أن إعطاء من علم أنه لا يؤمن يكون أفسد له على ما قررنا، وقد أعطاه فإذا فعل به ما هو الأفسد لا ما هو الأصلح. والثاني: أن ذلك إذا كان على هذا التقرير إفسادًا لا إصلاحًا وليس في مقدور الله تعالى سوى هذا، فلم يكن إذا في مقدوره الأصلح بل في مقدوره الأفسد، فإيجاب المعتزلة إياه ما لا قدرة له على تحصيله مع قولهم أن ليس الله تعالى ولاية تكليف العبد ما لا قدرة له عليه جهل عظيم.
Then there is no doubt that every disbeliever—God Almighty has given him, according to them [the Mu'tazila], the utmost of what is within His power of the most beneficial, and the disbeliever did not believe. So according to their view, God Almighty does not possess that through which there is rectification, nor power to rectify whomever He wills of His servants such that he would be rectified if He spent all that is in His treasuries. For there is nothing that He possesses through which there is rectification or over which He has power, except that with it, to that extent, there is corruption. Then no one is rectified by it. So how can it be plausible to impose something that He does not possess and over which He has no power? Since what He possesses does not bring about rectification, and that which does not bring about rectification is more beneficial than something else because they are equal in having no effect in attaining rectification; rather, giving it is more corruptive. Indeed, his disbelief and disobedience with it are uglier, and the punishment for it is severer. For disobedience with abundant deterrents and abandoning obedience with abundant blessings and incentives is uglier, and the deserving of punishment for it is stronger. After that, this statement is established from two aspects: First, that giving to one whom He knows will not believe is more corruptive for him, as we have established. And He has given him. So He did to him that which is more corruptive, not that which is most beneficial. Second, that if this, according to this explanation, is corruption, not rectification, and nothing is within the power of God Almighty except this, then the most beneficial is not within His power; rather, the more corruptive is within His power. So the Mu'tazila's obligation of that which He has no power to attain—while they say that God Almighty does not have the authority to charge a servant with what he has no power over—is great ignorance.
Source pages p. 1009

Commentary
This passage contains a two-fold argument against the Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-aslah (the most beneficial). The author first establishes that, according to the Mu'tazila, God gives each disbeliever the utmost possible benefit (aslah) within His power. Yet the disbeliever does not believe. The author then argues that if God had given more, it would have been corruptive, not beneficial, because the disbeliever's disobedience with more blessings would be uglier. Therefore, God's power is limited to corruptive acts when it comes to the disbeliever. The two aspects are: (1) Giving to one whom God knows will not believe is actually more harmful for him, so God's act is corruptive, not beneficial. (2) Since only corruptive acts are within God's power for such persons, the most beneficial is not within His power. The Mu'tazila's demand that God must do the most beneficial is thus an obligation of what He cannot do, contradicting their own principle that God does not charge a servant beyond his capacity. This shows their position to be ignorant.

Key Terms
الأصلح
الكافر
مقدور الله
الإفساد
الإصلاح
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-aslah requires God to give everyone the most beneficial, and that God can always do so.
Correction: The author shows that for disbelievers, giving more would be corruptive, not beneficial, so God cannot be obligated to give al-aslah; indeed, only corruptive acts are within His power for such persons.

Checkpoint

A) Because God does not want to give it.
B) Because giving more would only increase corruption and punishment.
C) Because the disbeliever rejects all benefits.
D) Because God's power is finite and He has exhausted it.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi's two-fold argument refutes the Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-aslah by showing that for disbelievers, giving more only corrupts, making the most beneficial impossible for God to provide.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The Mu'tazila thought God must give everyone the most beneficial to make them believe. But look at your own heart: Have you ever received a blessing and still disobeyed? How does that challenge any entitlement you feel toward God's favors?
?
If God gave you exactly what you thought was 'best' for you, do you think you would automatically obey? What does the lesson's argument about the disbeliever's persistent disbelief despite maximum benefit say about the role of your own free will and responsibility?

The Dilemma of Divine Power and al-Aslah
The author poses a dilemma to a Mu'tazilite regarding al-aslah and divine power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma that undermines the Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-Aslah by showing its contradiction with divine power.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilite al-Aslah conflicts with God's power to save or condemn.

In this passage, the author engages in a dialectical argument with a hypothetical Mu'tazilite opponent. He presents a dilemma that tests the consistency of the Mu'tazilite claim that God must always do what is most beneficial for creation. By considering the case of a disbeliever, the author reveals an internal contradiction in the doctrine, forcing the opponent to either admit that God withholds the best or that God lacks power—both unacceptable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage in Arabic and English
Begin by reading the Arabic text slowly, then refer to the literal translation to grasp the literal meaning. Note the dialectical style.
The author uses conditional statements (إن كان) to set up the dilemma.
2
Identify the two branches of the dilemma
The author asks: if God did all the most beneficial acts to this disbeliever now, would he believe? There are two possible answers: yes or no.
These branches lead to different consequences.
3
Analyze the consequence of each branch
If 'yes', the opponent admits God prevented the most beneficial by not doing it. If 'no', the opponent admits God lacks power to bring about faith.
Both consequences are problematic for the Mu'tazilite.
4
Examine the reframing question
The author then asks: is God able to do something more beneficial for this person now? If the opponent cannot affirm that, it implies God's power is limited.
This reinforces the dilemma.
5
Reflect on the final conclusion
The passage ends by stating that failing to posit a more beneficial act is a sign of exhaustion and incapacity to achieve the desired goal.
This directly challenges the Mu'tazilite position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأقول في جوابه وبالله التوفيق -: إن كان في قدرة الله تعالى وخزائن رحمته أمثال ما فعل من الأصلح فما قولك لو فعل ذلك في الحال بهذا الكافر هل يؤمن؟ فإن قال : نعم. فقد أقر بمنع الأصلح إذ لم يفعل ذلك كله ليؤمن، وإن قال : لا قيل فإذا لا قدرة له على ما به لو آمن من هذا العبد فحصل له صلاحه، فإذا السؤال أنه هل يقدر أن يفعل به في الوقت أصلح من هذا الذي فعل أم لا يصفه بالقدرة عليه؟ فينبغي أن يجيب عن هذا الحرف أو يعترض عليه، وإذا لم يكن عندك في مقدوره أن يفعل به في الوقت أصلح من هذا فهذا هو آية النفاد والعجز عن تحصيل المراد.
I say in response, and by God is success: If there are in the power of God Almighty and the treasuries of His mercy examples of what He did from the most beneficial, then what do you say if He did that to this disbeliever now—would he believe? If he says: Yes, then he has admitted the prevention of the most beneficial, since He did not do all of that to make him believe. And if he says: No, it is said: Then He has no power over that by which if this servant believed, his salvation would be obtained. So the question is: Is He able to do for him at this time something more beneficial than what He did, or do you not describe Him as having power over it? So he must answer this point or object to it. And if you have nothing in His power to do for him at this time more beneficial than this, then this is the sign of exhaustion and incapacity to achieve the desired goal.
Source pages p. 1010

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument. The author poses a dilemma to a Mu'tazilite who holds that God must always do what is most beneficial (al-aslah) and provide grace (al-lutf). The author asks: if God has in His power and treasuries of mercy examples of the most beneficial like what He actually did, then what do you say if He did all of that to this disbeliever now—would he believe? If the opponent answers 'yes,' then he admits that God prevented the most beneficial by not doing all of that to make him believe. If he answers 'no,' then God has no power to bring about faith for this servant, so his salvation cannot be achieved. Then the author reframes: is God able to do something more beneficial for him now than what He did, or do you not describe Him with power over it? If you cannot posit that God can do something more beneficial now, then that is a sign of limited power and incapacity to achieve the desired goal. The argument exposes a contradiction in the Mu'tazilite position.

Key Terms
أصلح
قدرة
إيمان
عجز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God must always do what is most beneficial for every individual.
Correction: This belief leads to a contradiction when applied to disbelievers: either God withholds the best or lacks power, both of which are inconsistent with Islamic theology.

Checkpoint
According to the author, if the Mu'tazilite says that God could make the disbeliever believe now by doing all the examples of the most beneficial, what does that imply?
A) God is just.
B) God prevented the most beneficial.
C) God is all-powerful.
D) The disbeliever is free.

Key Takeaway
The doctrine of al-Aslah cannot be consistently maintained without attributing either injustice or impotence to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you face a disappointement or trial, are you tempted to think that God is withholding something better from you? This lesson shows that either God lacks power or you are accusing Him of injustice. Which of these do you honestly struggle with, and how can you repent from it?
?
If you truly believe that God does not owe you anything, how does that change the way you react to unanswered prayers or unfulfilled desires? What specific attitude must you cultivate to align with the truth that God is not obligated to give you the 'best' in your eyes?

Denying Power over Possible Actions Implies Incapacity
The author argues that not describing God with power over the more beneficial implies incapacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that denying God power over any logically possible action—even if it does not occur—attributes incapacity to Him, based on the author's analogical argument.

Core Idea
Denying God any possible power implies attributing incapacity.

This passage continues the rebuttal of the Mu'tazilite position on divine power. The author uses a series of logical questions to show that restricting God's power to only what He does, and not what is more beneficial, inevitably leads to describing God with incapacity. Let's follow his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Notice the structure: a series of rhetorical questions and analogies.
The argument is dialectical, addressing a Mu'tazilite opponent.
2
Identify the two analogies
The first analogy concerns 'the more beneficial' (al-aslah). The second concerns 'grace' (lutf) for a person God knows will or will not believe. Find each and trace how they conclude that denying power implies incapacity.
Both analogies follow the same logical pattern.
3
Understand the logical structure
The author assumes that if something is logically possible, then attributing power over it is necessary; otherwise, it is incapacity. See how he makes the opponent admit this.
The key is that God's power is not limited by actual outcomes.
4
Connect to the broader doctrine
Reflect on why this matters: God's power is absolute. The Mu'tazilite view that God only does what is best (al-aslah) restricts His power. The author shows this leads to a contradiction.
This defends the Sunni view of unlimited divine power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال له : أليس من زعم أن الله تعالى يقدر أن يفعل في الوقت صلاحًا هو أصلح الأشياء للإنسان ولا يوصف بالقدرة على مثله يكون معجزا ربه؟ فلا بد من بلي. فقيل له: فإنك وإن أثبت قدرة على أمثال ما فعل فإذا لم تصفه بالقدرة على ما هو أصلح له أصلح له من هذا وصفته بالعجز أيضا ، ثم يقال له أليس أن من لم يصف الله تعالى بالقدرة على لطف يؤمن به من علم أنه يؤمن واصفًا له بالعجز؟ فإذا قال نعم قيل فكذا من لم يصف الله تعالى بالقدرة على لطف يؤمن به من علم أنه لا يؤمن به يكون واصفا له بالعجز كما أن من لم يصف الله تعالى بالقدرة على تحريك من يعلم أنه لا يحركه يكون واصفًا له بالعجز كمن لا يصفه بالقدرة على تحريك من يعلم أنه يحركه
Then it is said to him: Is not the one who claims that God Almighty is able to do at the time a good that is the most beneficial of things for man, but He is not described with power over its like, attributing incapacity to his Lord? So he must say yes. So it is said to him: For even if you affirm power over examples of what He did, if you do not describe Him with power over what is more beneficial for him from this, you describe Him with incapacity as well. Then it is said to him: Is not the one who does not describe God Almighty with power over a grace by which one who He knows will believe would believe, describing Him with incapacity? If he says yes, it is said: Likewise, the one who does not describe God Almighty with power over a grace by which one who He knows will not believe would believe is describing Him with incapacity, just as the one who does not describe God Almighty with power to move one whom He knows will not move is describing Him with incapacity, like the one who does not describe Him with power to move one whom He knows will move.
Source pages p. 1010

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the dialectical argument. The author poses a series of rhetorical questions to the Mu'tazilite opponent. First, he asks: Is not the one who claims that God can do something that is the most beneficial for man but denies that He can do its like, actually attributing incapacity to his Lord? The opponent must agree. Then the author argues: Even if you affirm that God has power over examples of what He did, if you do not describe Him as having power over what is more beneficial for him than this, you are describing Him with incapacity. Next, the author makes an analogy: Whoever does not describe God with power over a grace that would make a person whom God knows will believe actually believe is describing God with incapacity. Similarly, whoever does not describe God with power over a grace that would make a person whom God knows will not believe actually believe is also describing God with incapacity. This is like the case of someone who does not describe God with power to move a person whom He knows will not move—that person is describing God with incapacity, just as someone who does not describe God with power to move a person whom He knows will move would be. The point is that to deny God any power over something that is logically possible is to attribute incapacity to Him, regardless of whether the outcome is known to occur or not.

Key Terms
أصلح
قدرة
لطف
عجز
تحريك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying God power over what is not actual (e.g., a more beneficial action that He does not perform) does not imply incapacity.
Correction: The author argues that if an action is logically possible, then not attributing power over it to God necessarily implies incapacity, even if the action does not occur. God's power is not limited by actual outcomes.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does it mean to not describe God with power over a grace that would make a person who He knows will not believe become a believer?
A) It affirms God's mercy.
B) It describes God with incapacity.
C) It acknowledges human free will.
D) It shows God's wisdom.

Key Takeaway
Denying God power over any logically possible action, even if He does not do it, attributes incapacity to Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation that seems impossible, do you truly believe God has power over it, even if He chooses not to change it? Or do you unconsciously limit His power? How can you train your heart to affirm His complete power over all possibilities, even those you cannot see?
?
The author argues that denying God power over a grace that would make someone believe is attributing incapacity. Is there someone in your life whose guidance you have despaired of? Have you implicitly denied God's power to guide them? What would it mean for you to truly affirm His power over their hearts?
?
Consider the most beneficial thing God could do for you right now. Do you live as though God is able to do it, or do you act as though He is restricted? What could change in your dua and your reliance if you fully believed He has power over all possible goods?

The Dilemma of Combining Graces
The author examines whether combining multiple graces would achieve salvation, leading to a further dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dialectical argument that combining multiple graces either forces the Mu'tazilite to abandon the principle of aslah or implies that divine power is finite.

Core Idea
Combining graces reveals a dilemma: either God abandons the most beneficial or has finite power.

In this passage, the author continues his dialectical engagement with the Mu'tazilite position on divine grace and the most beneficial. He poses a critical question: if God has multiple examples of grace, what happens if He combines them with the actual grace? The answer leads to a dilemma that exposes contradictions in the Mu'tazilite framework and affirms the finitude of divine power over the most beneficial.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the use of conditional questions.
2
Identify the two branches of the dilemma
The first question has two possible answers: 'yes' or 'no'. Trace what each answer implies according to the author.
Focus on how each answer contradicts Mu'tazilite principles.
3
Examine the second question
The author then asks why God did not manifest those graces even if they wouldn't produce faith. Understand the opponent's objection and the author's response.
The response exposes the opponent's obfuscation.
4
Connect to the conclusion
See how the entire argument establishes that God cannot do what is more beneficial at the time, implying a limit on divine power.
This is the key doctrinal point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال له : إذا كان له أمثال فلو جمع تلك الأمثال لهذا اللطف الذي حصل في الوقت، هل يحصل به الصلاح أم لا؟ فإن قال نعم، فقد ترك قوله باللطف والأصلح ، وقيل :له كان جميع تلك الألطاف أصلح ولم يفعل فقد ترك الأصلح، وإن قال لا فلم يثبت بقدرته على تلك الأمثال قدرة على ما هو الأصلح لهذا الكافر من هذا الذي فعل وهو القول بتناهي القدرة، ثم يقال: هلا بين تلك الألطاف وإن كان لا يحصل بها الإيمان ليكون أشد تعريضا للإيمان كما فعل هذا اللطف، فإن قالوا: لو جمع بين هذا الأصلح الذي فعل وبين ما هو من أمثال لم يحصل بها الصلاح، بل يكون ذلك أفسد للكافر. قلنا وبهذا تبين تمويهكم في الاعتراض على فصل التعجيز وإثبات نهاية المقدور على ما قررنا. وتقرر بما أجبنا لكم أن لا يقدر فى الوقت أن يفعل ما هو الأصلح له مما فعل
Then it is said to him: If He has examples, then if He combined those examples with this grace that occurred now, would salvation be obtained by it or not? If he says yes, then he has abandoned his statement about grace and the most beneficial, and it is said: He had all those graces as more beneficial and did not do them, so he abandoned the most beneficial. And if he says no, then he has not established by His power over those examples power over what is more beneficial for this disbeliever than what He did, and that is the statement of the finitude of power. Then it is said: Why did He not make those graces manifest, even if faith would not be obtained by them, so that they would be a stronger inducement to faith, as this grace did? If they say: If He combined this most beneficial act with what are examples of it, salvation would not be obtained, but rather that would be more ruinous for the disbeliever. We say: And by this your obfuscation is exposed in objecting to the section on rendering incapable and establishing the limit of the possible, as we have established. And it is established by what we have answered you that He is not able at the time to do what is more beneficial for him than what He did.
Source pages p. 1011

Commentary
This passage is another dialectical step. The author asks the Mu'tazilite: If God has examples (amthal) of grace, suppose He combined all those examples with the grace that actually occurred—would salvation (salah) be achieved? If the opponent says yes, then he abandons his own principle of lutf and aslah, because God had all those graces available, which would have been more beneficial, yet He did not do them. If he says no, then he admits that God's power over those examples does not extend to what is more beneficial, implying that divine power is finite (tanahi al-qudra). Then the author asks: Why did God not manifest those graces, even if faith would not result, to make them a stronger inducement to faith, just as the actual grace was? The opponent might object that combining the actual most beneficial act with its examples would not bring salvation but rather cause more ruin for the disbeliever. The author responds: This exposes your obfuscation. It is established by our answer that God is not able at this time to do what is more beneficial for him than what He did.

لطف
أصلح
صلاح
تناهي القدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilite might think that combining multiple beneficial things always leads to a better outcome, so God should do so.
Correction: The argument shows that either God would then have abandoned the most beneficial (if yes) or that His power is finite (if no), undermining the Mu'tazilite position.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if the Mu'tazilite says that combining multiple graces would NOT achieve salvation, what does that imply?
A) That God is merciful.
B) That God's power is finite.
C) That the graces were not sufficient.
D) That the disbeliever is responsible.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite position on combining graces leads to a dilemma that either contradicts the principle of aslah or implies that divine power is finite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever secretly believed that God must give you everything you consider 'most beneficial' for your life? This passage exposes the contradiction in that demand. Right now, is there a blessing you are demanding from God that you think He owes you? Will you surrender that demand and trust His wisdom, even if it means accepting less than what you consider best?

The opponent ends up admitting that God cannot do what is more beneficial for the disbeliever at that time. Does this disturb your assumption that God's power is always unlimited in every way? When you face a situation where God seems not to give you what would be better, do you blame His power or His goodness? How can this passage recalibrate your trust in His perfect plan?
?
This argument forces a choice: either God abandoned the most beneficial act or His power is limited. Which horn of this dilemma unsettles you more? Today, will you consciously affirm that God's actions—even painful ones—are always perfectly wise and good, even when you cannot see the benefit?

Maturidi's Distinction Between the Beneficial for Faith and Medicinal Benefit
Maturidi explains why the beneficial for faith has no limit, unlike medicinal benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that spiritual benefit has no limit, unlike worldly medicinal benefit, and how the nature of the need determines the limit.

Core Idea
Spiritual benefit has no limit because the need it addresses is not measurable.

Maturidi builds upon Ash'ari's rebuttal by offering a deeper analysis. He distinguishes between two kinds of benefit: one that addresses a specific, measurable deficiency (like medicine) and one that addresses an infinite spiritual need. This passage explains why the latter can be added without harm.

Guided Path
1
Read Maturidi's Answer
Begin by reading the passage carefully. Notice Maturidi's initial statement that joining benefit to benefit does not harm, and his core distinction that spiritual benefit has no limit while worldly benefit does.
Pay attention to the contrast between 'salah' and 'naf' in the text.
2
Examine the Medicine Analogy
Maturidi defines benefit as removal of a measurable need. He uses fever as an example, showing how a specific dose of medicine restores balance, but excess cooling causes harm. This illustrates the limit of worldly benefit.
Consider why excess cooling is not beneficial but harmful.
3
Contrast the Two Types of Benefit
Reflect on how the measurable nature of worldly needs contrasts with spiritual needs, which are not limited. This is why adding more spiritual benefit never becomes harmful.
This is the key insight that refines the Ash'ari response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بهذا الجواب دفع أبو الحسن الأشعري كلامهم وأجاب الشيخ الإمام أبو منصور الماتريدي عن هذا الكلام فقال : إن انضم النفع إلى النفع لا يضر إنما يضر ضم ما ليس ينفع إلى النفع في التقدير وبه تقدير النفع للعليل، وها هنا القول إنه لا غاية لما به الصلاح وثم لما به النفع غاية؛ لذلك اختلف الأمران، وشرح هذا الكلام والكشف عنه: أن النفع في الحقيقة دفع الحاجة، والحاجة نقص يتمكن في الذات، وذلك كنقص الجوع وألم البرد والعلة الحادثة، ثم كل نقص في ذاته مقدر فإن الحمى مثلاً تحدث من غلبة الحرارة على البدن ولتلك الغلبة وزوال مزاج البدن عن الاعتدال، الذي أوجب هذا المرض قدر مقدر قد يكون قويًا وقد يكون ضعيفا، فقدر ما يغلب تلك الحرارة من المبردات ويعيد المزاج إلى الاعتدال معلوم، فإذا وجد تناول ذلك القدر في نفسه نفع على معنى أنه دفع الحرارة الغالبة التي أوجبت في البدن بعض المرض وزوال الاعتدال فإذا ضم إلى هذا القدر ما يزيد عليه فتلك الزيادة ليس عملها في رفع الحرارة التي حصل بها المرض؛ لأن ذلك حصل بالقدر المقدر بل عمل الزيادة في إثبات زيادة تبريد يزيل الاعتدال إذ فعله التبريد لا النفع وإنما يحصل النفع والضرر لاختلاف حال المحل فيحدث به مرض آخر ويحتاج
With this answer, Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari rebutted their speech, and the Shaykh Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi answered concerning this speech, saying: 'Benefit joined to benefit does not cause harm; rather, harm is caused only by joining what is not beneficial to the beneficial in estimation, and by it the estimation of benefit for the sick person [occurs]. And here the statement is that there is no limit to that by which [spiritual] benefit is achieved, whereas there is a limit to that by which [worldly] benefit is achieved; therefore the two matters differ.' And his explanation and elucidation: 'Benefit in reality is the removal of need, and need is a deficiency inherent in the essence, such as the deficiency of hunger, the pain of cold, and a disease that occurs. Then every deficiency in itself is measured; for example, fever occurs from the dominance of heat over the body, and for that dominance and the loss of the body's balance from moderation—which caused this disease—there is a specific measure, which may be strong or weak. So the measure that counteracts that heat with coolants and restores the balance to moderation is known. When that measure is taken, it is beneficial in the sense that it repels the dominant heat that caused some disease and loss of balance. If one adds to this measure what exceeds it, that excess does not act to remove the heat that caused the disease (since that was achieved by the proper measure), but rather the excess acts to establish additional cooling that destroys balance; for its action is cooling, not benefit. Benefit and harm only occur due to the different condition of the subject, and thus another disease occurs, and it needs...'
Source pages p. 1012

Commentary
This passage presents Abu Mansur al-Maturidi's own answer to the opponents' objection. Maturidi first acknowledges that Ash'ari's answer sufficed, but he offers a deeper distinction. He argues that the beneficial for spiritual salvation (al-salah) has no limit—adding more divine grace cannot cause harm. In contrast, worldly benefit (like medicine) has a specific limit, because it addresses a specific, measurable deficiency. He illustrates with the example of fever: a specific dose of cooling medicine restores balance; adding more disrupts balance and causes harm. Thus the medicine analogy does not apply to divine grace, which addresses an infinite need. This refines the Ash'ari response by grounding the difference in the nature of the needs being addressed.

Key Terms
النفع
الصلاح
الحاجة
التقدير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All forms of benefit are alike; adding more benefit can always cause harm if overdone.
Correction: Maturidi shows that spiritual benefit (al-salah) is not a removal of a measurable deficiency, so adding more does not cause harm; it is fundamentally different from medicinal benefit.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is there no limit to what brings about spiritual benefit (al-salah)?
A) Because God's power is infinite.
B) Because spiritual need is not measurable.
C) Because the beneficial for faith is not a removal of deficiency.
D) Because the example of medicine proves it.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's argument establishes that spiritual benefit is not subject to the same constraints as physical benefits because the needs it addresses are not finite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Think of a time when you kept adding something you thought was good (like working more hours to earn more) and found it actually harmed your health or relationships. What specific step will you take to stop adding 'excess' in a worldly area this week?
?

Where in your life are you settling for 'just enough' prayer, charity, or repentance, instead of seeking more without fear? Write down one specific way you will push beyond your comfort zone to add more spiritual goodness today.
?
Maturidi shows that taking the exact needed measure of medicine restores balance, but excess destroys it. Where in your spiritual life might you be 'overdosing'—doing more of something that seems good but is actually throwing your balance off (e.g., excessive fasting, over-engagement in debates)? What is the 'proper measure' for you right now, and how will you identify it?
