Al-Maturidi on Finite Medical Benefit vs Infinite Religious Righteousness
Al-Maturidi explains the limit of medical benefit versus the limitlessness of religious righteousness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why religious righteousness has no finite limit whereas worldly benefits like medicine have a precise measure that cannot be exceeded without causing harm.

Core Idea
Righteousness is infinitely augmentable; medicine has a fixed dose beyond which it becomes harmful.

Al-Maturidi presents a crucial argument distinguishing the finite nature of physical remedies from the limitless nature of religious merit. This passage explains why the analogy of medicine fails when applied to divine reward, and why the causes of righteousness are without end.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage along with its English translation. Notice the structure: first a general statement, then a detailed medical example, and finally the contrast with righteousness.
2
Understand the medical example
Focus on the explanation of fever and the measured dose of cooling medicine. Why does adding extra medicine become harmful? Because it no longer removes the original heat but introduces a new imbalance.
The key concept is 'estimated measure' (qadr muqaddar).
3
Contrast with righteousness
Now see how al-Maturidi applies the same logic to righteousness: it has no limit because any righteousness can be surpassed by a greater one. There is no 'overdose' of righteousness.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this distinction matters for theology: it refutes those who analogize divine actions to human practices, showing that God's reward is not bound by finite measures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بهذا الجواب دفع أبو الحسن الأشعري كلامهم وأجاب الشيخ الإمام أبو منصور الماتريدي عن هذا الكلام فقال : إن انضم النفع إلى النفع لا يضر إنما يضر ضم ما ليس ينفع إلى النفع في التقدير وبه تقدير النفع للعليل، وها هنا القول إنه لا غاية لما به الصلاح وثم لما به النفع غاية؛ لذلك اختلف الأمران، وشرح هذا الكلام والكشف عنه: أن النفع في الحقيقة دفع الحاجة، والحاجة نقص يتمكن في الذات، وذلك كنقص الجوع وألم البرد والعلة الحادثة، ثم كل نقص في ذاته مقدر فإن الحمى مثلاً تحدث من غلبة الحرارة على البدن ولتلك الغلبة وزوال مزاج البدن عن الاعتدال، الذي أوجب هذا المرض قدر مقدر قد يكون قويًا وقد يكون ضعيفا، فقدر ما يغلب تلك الحرارة من المبردات ويعيد المزاج إلى الاعتدال معلوم، فإذا وجد تناول ذلك القدر في نفسه نفع على معنى أنه دفع الحرارة الغالبة التي أوجبت في البدن بعض المرض وزوال الاعتدال فإذا ضم إلى هذا القدر ما يزيد عليه فتلك الزيادة ليس عملها في رفع الحرارة التي حصل بها المرض؛ لأن ذلك حصل بالقدر المقدر بل عمل الزيادة في إثبات زيادة تبريد يزيل الاعتدال إذ فعله التبريد لا النفع وإنما يحصل النفع والضرر لاختلاف حال المحل فيحدث به مرض آخر ويحتاج إلى دفعه فكانت الزيادة ضارة لا نافعة، فأما إلى القدر الذي يقاوم الحرارة فكل جزء منه يدفع شيئًا من أجزاء الحرارة فيكون دافعا للبعض فيكون نافعًا فهذا معنى قوله - رحمه الله -: أن ضم النفع إلى النفع لا يضر إنما يضر ضم ما ليس ينفع إلى النفع فأما ما هو صلاح في الدين فليس له حد ولا نهاية إذ ما من صلاح إلا ويتوهم ما هو أصلح منه ، فإذا لم يكن للصلاح في نفسه نهاية لا يتصور حصول ضد الصلاح بالمجاوزة عن القدر المقدر له بل كلما ضم صلاح إلى صلاح ازداد الصلاح، وإذا لم يكن للصلاح نهاية لم يكن لأسباب الصلاح نهاية
By this answer Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari repelled their speech, and the Shaykh Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi answered this speech saying: 'If benefit (al-naf') is joined to benefit it does not harm; rather, harm comes from joining what is not beneficial to the beneficial in estimation (taqdīr) and by it the estimation of benefit for the sick person. And here the statement is that there is no limit for that by which righteousness (al-ṣalāḥ) [is achieved], while for that by which benefit is [achieved] there is a limit; therefore the two matters differ.' And the explanation of this speech and its unveiling is: that benefit in reality is the removal of need, and need is a deficiency occurring in the self, such as the deficiency of hunger and the pain of cold and an incident ailment. Then every deficiency in itself is estimated (muqaddar). For example, fever occurs from the dominance of heat over the body, and for that dominance and the removal of the body's mixture from equilibrium there is an estimated measure (qadr muqaddar) that may be strong or weak. So the measure that overcomes that heat from cooling agents and restores the mixture to equilibrium is known. When that measure is taken, it is in itself beneficial in the sense that it removes the dominant heat that caused some illness and loss of equilibrium. Then if something extra is joined to this measure, that extra's action is not in removing the heat that caused the illness, because that was achieved by the estimated measure; rather, the extra's action is in establishing additional cooling that removes equilibrium, since its action is cooling, not benefit. Benefit and harm occur only due to the differing state of the locus, so it causes another illness and needs to be removed. Thus the extra becomes harmful, not beneficial. As for the measure that counteracts the heat, every part of it repels some part of the heat, so it repels some and is beneficial. This is the meaning of his saying - may Allah have mercy on him -: 'Joining benefit to benefit does not harm; rather harm comes from joining what is not beneficial to the beneficial.' As for what is righteousness in religion, it has no limit or end, because there is no righteousness except that one can conceive of something more righteous than it. So when righteousness in itself has no end, it is not conceivable that the opposite of righteousness (i.e., harm) would occur by exceeding an estimated measure for it; rather, whenever righteousness is joined to righteousness, righteousness increases. And if righteousness has no end, then the causes of righteousness have no end.
Source pages p. 1012

Commentary
This passage presents a key argument distinguishing between worldly benefit and religious righteousness. The author, Imam al-Maturidi, begins by affirming that joining benefit to benefit does not cause harm; harm arises only when something non-beneficial is added to the beneficial. In the case of medicine, there is a precise measure that counteracts the illness, and exceeding that measure causes harm because the extra portion does not continue the beneficial effect but instead introduces a new imbalance. Al-Maturidi then contrasts this with righteousness (al-ṣalāḥ) in religion: it has no limit or end because any given righteousness can be surpassed by a greater one. There is no fixed 'dose' beyond which exceeding it would produce the opposite effect; rather, righteousness is infinitely augmentable. Consequently, the causes of righteousness are also without limit. This argument refutes those who analogize divine actions to human medical practices, showing that the parallel fails because the nature of religious merit is fundamentally different from finite physical remedies. Al-Maturidi follows the order: first stating the opponent's objection, then giving al-Ash'ari's initial response, then expanding with his own detailed explanation of measurement limits in medicine, and finally applying the principle to the realm of righteousness.

Key Terms
النفع (al-naf')
الضر (al-ḍarr)
الصلاح (al-ṣalāḥ)
القدر المقدر (al-qadr al-muqaddar)
الحاجة (al-ḥāja)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because beneficial things like medicine become harmful when exceeded, the same applies to religious acts (e.g., too much prayer is harmful).
Correction: This passage clarifies that righteousness has no limit; it can always be increased without causing harm, unlike physical remedies which have a fixed beneficial dose.

Checkpoint
According to al-Maturidi, why does joining extra medicine to the beneficial dose become harmful?
A) Because the extra medicine is always poisonous.
B) Because the extra medicine does not remove the original illness but instead adds a new imbalance.
C) Because Allah decrees harm for any excess.
D) Because the patient becomes immune to the medicine.

Key Takeaway
Righteousness in religion is not limited by a fixed measure and can always be increased, unlike physical remedies which have a precise beneficial dose beyond which they become harmful.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what area of your religious practice have you been treating righteousness like medicine—thinking there is a point where 'enough is enough'? Where has that assumption stopped you from seeking greater closeness to Allah?
?
When you reflect on your deeds, do you feel satisfied with a certain level of righteousness, or are you constantly seeking to augment it? What is holding you back from that increase?
?
If righteousness has no limit, what one concrete step will you take today to add righteousness to your righteousness, knowing that no added good can ever become harmful?

Refuting Limitation of Divine Power and the Dialectical Method
Al-Maturidi refutes limiting causes and explains his dialectical concessions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why limiting the causes of righteousness implies divine incapacity and how al-Maturidi uses dialectical concessions to refute opponents without affirming secondary causality.

Core Idea
Apparent concessions are strategic; only Allah is the true agent of harm and benefit.

Al-Maturidi confronts a challenge: if we say that the causes of righteousness have a fixed limit, does that limit apply to Allah's power? He answers with a firm 'no'—and then reveals a key insight about his own method. Often, he concedes points he does not believe, not out of doubt, but as a dialectical weapon to expose the flaws in his opponents' reasoning. This passage shows you both the doctrine and the strategy behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic and translation once to grasp the flow. Notice how the argument moves from refutation to methodological clarification to supporting examples from the Companions.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two main claims
First, find the refutation of limiting causes (and why that implies 'ajz). Second, locate the explanation of taslīm jadal and its purpose.
These two claims are intertwined; the second explains the first's apparent concession.
3
Analyze the dialectical method
Look at how al-Maturidi says he 'concedes' then 'shows the corruption of their inference.' Ask: why would this be more effective than a direct denial?
This method is common in kalām; it uses the opponent's own assumptions.
4
Reflect on the examples from Abu Bakr and 'Umar
These stories illustrate that the true agent of harm/benefit is Allah alone. How do they reinforce the refutation of limiting divine power?
5
Connect to the final sentence
The passage ends with 'righteousness increases, truthfulness does not.' Think about how this relates to the unbounded nature of divine power.
This may also hint at the difference between actions and attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمن أثبت لأسباب الصلاح الداخلة تحت قدرة الباري جل وعلا نهاية فقد قال بنهاية مقدوره وهو إثبات العجز وهو باطل، وليس هذا من الشيخ أبي منصور بتسليم أن الدواء نافع بل النافع هو الله تعالى إلا أنه جعل سببًا للنفع غير أنه - من دأبه أنه كثيرًا ما يسلم تسليم جدل ما لا يقول به ثم يبين مع فساد استدلال الخصوم ليكون ذلك أبين الضلالتهم وجهالتهم وأغيظ لهم، وكيف نسلم ذلك وقد ثبت أن الضار والنافع في الحقيقة هو الله تعالى، وقد ذكر ذلك – رحمه الله - في مواضع من كتبه وقد روى عن أبي بكر الصديق عنه أنه قال : الطبيب أمرضني، وروى عن عمر الله أنه نهى عن أكل الجبن وقيل له : إنه يضر؟ فقال: لو كنت أعلم أنه يضرني لعبدته وهذا إخبار منه الله أن الضار في الحقيقة هو الله ، تعالى إلا أنه جرى معهم في الظاهر وأراهم بطلان ما يتعلقون به لو كان الأمر على ما يزعمون وهذا طريقه لأهل النظر مسلوكة، والله الموفق. رحمه الله الصلاح يتزايد والصدق لا يتزايد
Whoever establishes a limit for the causes of righteousness that fall under the power of the Creator, Mighty and Majestic, has indeed spoken of a limit to His power, and that is an affirmation of incapacity, which is false. And this is not from the Shaykh Abu Mansur [meaning his concession is not real]; rather, the Benefactor is Allah Most High, except that He made [the medicine] a cause for benefit. However, it is his habit that he often concedes in dialectical concession (taslīm jadal) what he does not actually hold, then he shows the corruption of the opponents' inference, so that it becomes more evident that they are astray and ignorant, and more vexing to them. How could we concede that, when it has been established that the truly harmful and beneficial is Allah Most High? He – may Allah have mercy on him – has mentioned this in places in his books, and it has been narrated from Abu Bakr al-Siddiq that he said: 'The physician made me ill.' And it has been narrated from 'Umar [ibn al-Khattab] that he forbade eating cheese, and it was said to him: 'It is harmful?' He said: 'If I knew that it harms me, I would worship it.' This is an informing from him that the truly harmful is Allah. However, he went along with them in appearance and showed them the invalidity of what they cling to, if the matter were as they claim. And this method is followed by people of theoretical inquiry. And Allah is the Granter of success. May Allah have mercy on him. Righteousness increases, and truthfulness does not increase.
Source pages p. 1013

Commentary
This passage serves a dual purpose: first, it refutes any suggestion that the causes of righteousness can be limited, since that would imply a limitation on Allah's power, which is impossible. Second, it clarifies the author's dialectical method. Al-Maturidi explains that his apparent concession that medicine is beneficial and has a fixed measure is not a real affirmation of causality or inherent efficacy in the medicine. Rather, he only concedes such points as a dialectical stratagem (taslīm jadal) to argue from the opponent's premises and demonstrate the inconsistency of their reasoning. He reaffirms the Ash'ari-Maturidi principle that the true agent of benefit and harm is Allah alone. He supports this with reports from Abu Bakr al-Siddiq and 'Umar ibn al-Khattab, who recognized that even apparent causes like a physician or cheese do not truly bring benefit or harm—only Allah does. He then returns to the original theme, concluding that righteousness (al-ṣalāḥ) increases without limit, whereas truthfulness (al-ṣidq) does not increase (possibly meaning it is a single quality that cannot be intensified). The passage thus reiterates the unbounded nature of divine power and the proper understanding of causality in Islam.

Key Terms
الصلاح (al-ṣalāḥ)
نهاية (nihāya)
مقدور (maqdūr)
العجز (al-ʿajz)
تسليم جدل (taslīm jadal)
الضار (al-ḍār)
النافع (al-nāfiʿ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that al-Maturidi's concession about medicine being beneficial means he believes in secondary causality or that causes have independent efficacy.
Correction: Al-Maturidi clarifies that such concessions are purely dialectical (taslīm jadal) – he temporarily accepts the opponent's premise to argue against them, but his actual doctrine is that Allah alone is the true agent of benefit and harm.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Maturidi sometimes concede points he does not actually believe?
A) Because he is unsure of the truth.
B) To better expose the opponents' error and show their ignorance.
C) Because he follows the Ash'ari school of taqlid.
D) To avoid lengthy debate.

Key Takeaway
Limiting the causes of righteousness implies limiting Allah's power, which is false; al-Maturidi's apparent concessions are solely dialectical strategies to unmask opponents' errors, while the true agent of all benefit and harm is Allah alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you rely on a doctor or take medicine, does your heart truly believe that only Allah causes healing, or do you see the medicine as the real agent? Examine your own tawhid in daily matters. What would it take for you to reach the conviction of Abu Bakr who said 'The physician made me ill,' meaning full attention to Allah as the real cause? Commit to a practice of reminding yourself before taking any means.
?
Al-Maturidi used strategic concessions in debate to expose errors. In your own discussions about religion, do you ever pretend to accept a false premise to later reveal its inconsistency, or do you insist on being right immediately? This lesson challenges you to learn wisdom in debate. Can you humbly use strategy for the sake of truth, or does your ego get in the way?
?
The lesson ends with 'Righteousness increases, truthfulness does not increase.' Reflect: Are you more concerned with increasing your good deeds (quantity) or with the truthfulness of your heart (quality)? Which one are you neglecting? What change will you make today to prioritize inner sincerity over external actions?

Divine Power Extends to Infinite Goodness
Nasafi argues that God's power over goodness is unlimited using contrast, evidence, and analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's argument that God's power extends to infinite goodness, not being limited to any finite degree, through the use of contrast and analogy with the indivisible atom.

Core Idea
Divine power over goodness is infinite, as goodness itself increases without bound.

Nasafi addresses a potential misunderstanding: if God's power is infinite, can He create a truth truer than the Quran or something smaller than an atom? He explains these are impossible due to the nature of truth and matter. However, goodness is different—it increases inherently. Restricting God's power over goodness would imply incapability. Then he uses the opponent's concept of the indivisible atom to demonstrate that even if a minimum goodness exists, God can increase it infinitely.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Let the argument unfold naturally.
Pay attention to the flow from contrast to evidence to analogy.
2
Identify the two contrasting cases
Notice how Nasafi contrasts truth/atom (which do not increase) with goodness (which increases). Understand why power over the former is not claimed while the latter implies infinite power.
3
Understand the observational evidence
Nasafi provides examples: fulfilling all needs vs. one, and lesser worship with greater reward. Recognize this as proof that goodness inherently increases.
He says denying this is denying the observable.
4
Analyze the analogy with the indivisible atom
Study how he uses the opponent's own concept to show that even if there is a minimum goodness, God can add more infinitely, just as He can add parts to the atom.
This is the core of his argument against limiting divine power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بإثبات النهاية لمقدوره، فإن أحدًا لا يقول الله تعالى قادر على صدق أصدق من القرآن ولا على أصغر من الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ، وهذا لأنا بينا أن الصلاح يتزايد والصدق لا يتزايد، وكذا أصغر من الجزء محال وجوده، فكان إثبات القدرة على ذلك إثباتًا للقدرة على المحال، وفيما نحن فيه الأمر بخلافه إذ هو متزايد في نفسه، وقصر القدرة على قدر مقدر مما لا يتزايد في نفسه إثبات للعجز وراءه كمن يقول إن الله تعالى لا يقدر على أن يزيد على العالم شيئًا ولا أن يخلق لزيد ولدًا آخر، والدليل على كون الصلاح متزايد في نفسه أن من رد كل حاجة لإنسان كان ذلك أصلح له من أن يقضي له حاجة واحدة، وكذا لو كان الله تعالى افترض من العبادات أقل مما افترض وجعل ثوابها أكثر مما جعل، لا شك أن ذلك كان أصلح، على أن من أنكر كون المصلحة متزايدة في نفسها فقد أنكر المشاهدة، وإذا ثبت تزايدها كان قصر القدرة على قدر منها مقدر إثباتا للعجز عما وراءه. ثم نقول له: فصل الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ دليل خصمك لو عقلت، فإن خصمك يقول كما أثبتنا الابتداء منتهيًا إلى ما لا أصغر منه فكذلك نثبت الابتداء في الصلاح منتهيًا إلى حد لا يكون في الصلاح أقل منه، ثم كما أن جزءًا لا يتجزأ لا يزاد عليه جزء إلا والله تعالى قدرة أن يزيد عليه أجزاء إلى ما لا يتناهى، فكذلك ما من صلاح يوجد إلا والله تعالى عليه من القدرة أن يزيد عليه آخر إلى ما لا يتناهى فصار القول بتناهي الصلاح وسلب قدرة الله تعالى على أن يزيد على قدر مقدر تعجيزا له كما في أجزاء الجسم كان القول ببلوغ الأجزاء غاية لا يقدر الله تعالى على الزيادة عليها تعجيزا، والله الموفق.
By affirming an end to His power, for no one says that God Most High is able to produce a truth truer than the Quran, nor to create something smaller than the indivisible atom. This is because we have shown that goodness increases while truth does not increase, and likewise the smaller than the atom is impossible in existence. So affirming power over that would be affirming power over the impossible. But in our case, the matter is contrary to that, because goodness increases in itself. Restricting power to a limited amount of what does not increase in itself is an affirmation of incapability beyond it, like one who says that God Most High is not able to add anything to the world nor to create another son for Zayd. The evidence that goodness increases in itself is that if someone fulfills every need of a person, that is more beneficial for him than fulfilling one need. Likewise, if God Most High had prescribed fewer acts of worship than He prescribed and made their reward greater than He made, there is no doubt that would have been more beneficial. Moreover, whoever denies that benefit increases in itself has denied what is observed. When its increase is established, restricting power to a limited amount of it is an affirmation of incapability beyond it. Then we say to him: Consider the indivisible atom, the argument of your opponent if you understood. For your opponent says: Just as we have established a beginning terminating at that which has no smaller, so we establish a beginning in goodness terminating at a limit below which there is no lesser goodness. Then just as no part is added to the indivisible atom without God Most High having the power to add parts to it ad infinitum, so also there is no goodness that exists except that God Most High has the power to add another to it ad infinitum. Thus the claim that goodness is finite and the denial of God’s power to increase beyond a limited amount is an attribution of incapability to Him, just as in the parts of the body, the claim that the parts reach a limit beyond which God Most High cannot increase is an attribution of incapability. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 1014

Commentary
This passage is a proof that God's power is not limited to a finite degree of goodness. The author begins by contrasting two cases: no one claims God can produce a truth truer than the Quran or create something smaller than the indivisible atom, because truth does not increase and the atom is minimal. In those cases, claiming power over them would be over the impossible. However, goodness (salah) is different because it increases in itself. Restricting divine power to a limited amount of what increases is essentially attributing incapability beyond that limit, just as if one said God cannot add to the world. The evidence for the increase of goodness is observational: fulfilling all needs is more beneficial than one; a lesser obligation with greater reward would be more beneficial. Denying this increase is denying the observable. Once increase is established, any restriction is a limitation of power. The author then uses the opponent's own concept of the indivisible atom (juz' la yatajaza') to make an analogy: just as the opponent posits a smallest atom beyond which no smaller exists, yet admits God can add parts to it infinitely, similarly in goodness, even if there is a minimum, God can still add greater goodness without end. Therefore, claiming goodness has a finite limit and that God's power stops there is equivalent to claiming that physical parts reach a limit God cannot exceed — both are attributions of incapability.

Key Terms
صلاح
مصلحة
قدرة
جزء لا يتجزأ
تعجيز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's power is limited only to what actually exists or occurs, and that goodness has a finite maximum.
Correction: Nasafi shows that goodness inherently increases, so God's power must extend to infinite degrees of goodness, just as He can add parts to the atom indefinitely.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the author use to argue that God's power over goodness is not limited?
A) The creation of the world
B) The indivisible atom (juz' la yatajaza')
C) The truth of the Quran
D) The reward of worship

Key Takeaway
Divine power over goodness is not limited to any finite degree because goodness itself is infinitely extensible, as shown by observation and the atom analogy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that Allah's power over goodness is unlimited, even when your own experience tells you that some good things are 'impossible'? What specific dua have you abandoned because you thought it was too much to ask?
?
If Allah can add goodness without end, why do you settle for a mediocre relationship with Him? What is one area of your life where you have limited your expectations of His mercy, and what would it look like to trust His infinite power there?
?
The analogy of the atom shows that even at the smallest level, Allah can add more. How does this change the way you view the 'small' good deeds you do? Will you commit to doing one extra good deed today, believing that Allah can multiply its benefit infinitely?

Refuting the Claim about Power over Truth
Nasafi refutes the claim that God lacks power over a truth truer than the Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi argues that God's truth and the Quran are not subject to power as claimed by the opponents.

Core Idea
Divine truth and the Quran are above being subject to power.

Nasafi begins by addressing a specific objection: that God cannot be described as having power over a truth truer than the Quran. He dismisses this, explaining that divine truth and the Quran are not the kind of things that are subject to power. This lesson clarifies that foundational point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation to understand the objection and Nasafi's response.
Notice the phrase 'غير داخل تحت القدرة'.
2
Identify the key claim
Notice how Nasafi separates divine truth and the Quran from being objects of power.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this refutation is important for the broader discussion on divine attributes.
It establishes that divine speech is eternal and not contingent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ما يزعمون أن الله تعالى لا يوصف بالقدرة على صدق أصدق من القرآن شيء بنوه على أصلهم الفاسد، فأما صدق الله تعالى فغير داخل تحت القدرة، وكذا القرآن على ما مر. ثم نقول لهم : أليس أن الله تعالى كان قادرًا على أن يميت قبل البلوغ كل من يعلم منه الكفر بعد البلوغ؟ فإن قالوا: لا. فقد عجزوه، وإن قالوا: نعم. فقد أثبتوا له القدرة على الأصلح إذا كانت الإماتة أصلح له في هذه الحالة لما يحصل له بها النجاة من الخلود في أسفل دركات النيران، وإن عادوا إلى تجاهلهم أن المعترض لا على المنزلتين كان له أصلح - عدنا إلى ما أجبنا، وكذا الله تعالى قادر على أن لا يركب فيهم العقول. ثم نقول : أليس أن الله تعالى أعطى الكافر قدرة الكفر ؟ فلا بد من بلى. قيل فأي الأمرين أصلح أن لا يعطى له القدرة إذا علم أنه يكفر أو أن يعطى القدرة؟ فإن قال الأصلح أن لا يعطي له القدرة. قلنا : فإذا أعطى فقد ترك الأصلح، وكذا إذا كان قادرا أن لا يعطي فكان إذا قادرًا على أن يفعل به ما هو الأصلح ولم يفعل إذا أعطى القدرة. وإن قال: الأصلح له أن يعطى القدرة مع علمه أنه يكفر بها لا محالة، فقد كابر وعاند. وإن عادوا إلى فصل التعريض ، فقد سبق الكلام فيه . وإن قالوا: لو لم يعطه القدرة لم يبق مكلفا. قلنا وأي ضرر عليه بخروجه عن التكليف بل له فيه أعظم نفع حيث لم يستحق العذاب بالمؤبد.
Then what they claim — that God Most High is not described as having power over a truth truer than the Quran — they build on their corrupt principle. As for the truth of God Most High, it is not subject to power, and likewise the Quran as has passed. Then we say to them: Is it not that God Most High was able to cause to die, before reaching maturity, every one of whom He knows that he would disbelieve after reaching maturity? If they say: No, then they have attributed incapability to Him. If they say: Yes, then they have affirmed for Him power over the optimal (al-aslah), since causing death is more optimal for him in this state because it brings him salvation from eternal abiding in the lowest depths of the Fire. If they return to their ignorance that the objector has no optimal [for the two states], we return to what we answered. And likewise, God Most High is able not to place intellects in them. Then we say: Is it not that God Most High gave the disbeliever the power of disbelief? They must answer yes. It is said: Which of the two is more optimal: that He does not give him the power when He knows he will disbelieve, or that He gives him the power? If he says: The optimal is that He does not give him the power. We say: Then if He gives, He has abandoned the optimal. And likewise, since He is able not to give, He is then able to do what is more optimal for him and did not do it when He gave the power. If he says: The optimal for him is that He gives him the power despite knowing that he will inevitably disbelieve through it, then he has acted obstinately and stubbornly. If they return to the topic of indirect expression (ta'rid), that discussion has already passed. If they say: If He did not give him the power, he would not remain a morally responsible agent (mukallaf). We say: What harm is there to him in leaving moral responsibility? Rather, in that there is a great benefit for him, since he would not deserve eternal punishment.
Source pages p. 1015

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical refutation of objections regarding divine power and optimal goodness (al-aslah). The author addresses several points. First, he dismisses the claim that God cannot be described as having power over a truth truer than the Quran, noting that divine truth and the Quran are not subject to power in that sense. Then, he raises a question: Could God have caused every person He knows will disbelieve after puberty to die before puberty? If the opponent says no, they ascribe incapability to God; if yes, they affirm God's power to do the optimal since death saves such a person from eternal hellfire. The opponent might evade by claiming there is no optimal in such cases, but the author asserts he has already answered that. God is also able not to grant intellect to such people. Next, he asks about the disbeliever’s power to disbelieve: If God is able not to give that power, which is more optimal? If the opponent says not giving it is optimal, then giving it abandons the optimal. If the opponent says giving it is optimal despite knowledge of disbelief, that is obstinate. If they bring up the topic of ta'rid (indirect expression), it was already discussed. Finally, if they argue that without the power to disbelieve the person would not be morally responsible (mukallaf), the author replies that liberation from responsibility is a great benefit, saving one from eternal torment.

Key Terms
صدق
قدرة
قرآن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God can have power over a truth truer than the Quran, implying the Quran is not the highest truth.
Correction: Nasafi explains that divine truth and the Quran are not subject to power because they are eternal and beyond being objects of power.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is God not described as having power over a truth truer than the Quran?
A) Because such a truth does not exist
B) Because divine truth and the Quran are not subject to power
C) Because God is incapable of it
D) Because the opponents built it on a sound principle

Key Takeaway
Divine truth and the Quran are eternal and not subject to divine power in the sense of being created or alterable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran, do you approach it as a text subject to your own reasoning and critique, or do you acknowledge that its truth is above your ability to judge? What would change in your reading if you truly believed it is beyond your power to alter or question?
?
If God's truth is not subject to power, what does that mean for your attempts to 'prove' the Quran's truth or defend it with your own arguments? Are you relying on your own power or submitting to its inherent truth?
?
Have you ever felt you could add to or improve upon the message of the Quran in your understanding? How does Nasafi's refutation challenge that assumption?

Causing Death Before Puberty to Prevent Disbelief
Nasafi questions whether God could cause potential disbelievers to die before puberty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that if God can cause the death of potential disbelievers before puberty, then He has power over what is optimal for them.

Core Idea
God's ability to cause death before puberty proves His power over the optimal.

In this part of the debate, Nasafi poses a question to his opponents: Could God have caused every person He knows will disbelieve after puberty to die before puberty? The response reveals a key point about divine power and optimal goodness.

Guided Path
1
Follow the dialectical question
Read the question Nasafi asks and note the two possible answers.
The question is about causing death before puberty.
2
Analyse the implications
If the opponent says no, they imply incapability in God. If yes, they affirm God's power over the optimal.
3
Consider the opponent's evasion
Notice how the opponent might claim there is no optimal in such cases, and Nasafi's response that it was already answered.
Nasafi also adds that God could not place intellects in them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ما يزعمون أن الله تعالى لا يوصف بالقدرة على صدق أصدق من القرآن شيء بنوه على أصلهم الفاسد، فأما صدق الله تعالى فغير داخل تحت القدرة، وكذا القرآن على ما مر. ثم نقول لهم : أليس أن الله تعالى كان قادرًا على أن يميت قبل البلوغ كل من يعلم منه الكفر بعد البلوغ؟ فإن قالوا: لا. فقد عجزوه، وإن قالوا: نعم. فقد أثبتوا له القدرة على الأصلح إذا كانت الإماتة أصلح له في هذه الحالة لما يحصل له بها النجاة من الخلود في أسفل دركات النيران، وإن عادوا إلى تجاهلهم أن المعترض لا على المنزلتين كان له أصلح - عدنا إلى ما أجبنا، وكذا الله تعالى قادر على أن لا يركب فيهم العقول. ثم نقول : أليس أن الله تعالى أعطى الكافر قدرة الكفر ؟ فلا بد من بلى. قيل فأي الأمرين أصلح أن لا يعطى له القدرة إذا علم أنه يكفر أو أن يعطى القدرة؟ فإن قال الأصلح أن لا يعطي له القدرة. قلنا : فإذا أعطى فقد ترك الأصلح، وكذا إذا كان قادرا أن لا يعطي فكان إذا قادرًا على أن يفعل به ما هو الأصلح ولم يفعل إذا أعطى القدرة. وإن قال: الأصلح له أن يعطى القدرة مع علمه أنه يكفر بها لا محالة، فقد كابر وعاند. وإن عادوا إلى فصل التعريض ، فقد سبق الكلام فيه . وإن قالوا: لو لم يعطه القدرة لم يبق مكلفا. قلنا وأي ضرر عليه بخروجه عن التكليف بل له فيه أعظم نفع حيث لم يستحق العذاب بالمؤبد.
Then what they claim — that God Most High is not described as having power over a truth truer than the Quran — they build on their corrupt principle. As for the truth of God Most High, it is not subject to power, and likewise the Quran as has passed. Then we say to them: Is it not that God Most High was able to cause to die, before reaching maturity, every one of whom He knows that he would disbelieve after reaching maturity? If they say: No, then they have attributed incapability to Him. If they say: Yes, then they have affirmed for Him power over the optimal (al-aslah), since causing death is more optimal for him in this state because it brings him salvation from eternal abiding in the lowest depths of the Fire. If they return to their ignorance that the objector has no optimal [for the two states], we return to what we answered. And likewise, God Most High is able not to place intellects in them. Then we say: Is it not that God Most High gave the disbeliever the power of disbelief? They must answer yes. It is said: Which of the two is more optimal: that He does not give him the power when He knows he will disbelieve, or that He gives him the power? If he says: The optimal is that He does not give him the power. We say: Then if He gives, He has abandoned the optimal. And likewise, since He is able not to give, He is then able to do what is more optimal for him and did not do it when He gave the power. If he says: The optimal for him is that He gives him the power despite knowing that he will inevitably disbelieve through it, then he has acted obstinately and stubbornly. If they return to the topic of indirect expression (ta'rid), that discussion has already passed. If they say: If He did not give him the power, he would not remain a morally responsible agent (mukallaf). We say: What harm is there to him in leaving moral responsibility? Rather, in that there is a great benefit for him, since he would not deserve eternal punishment.
Source pages p. 1015

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical refutation of objections regarding divine power and optimal goodness (al-aslah). The author addresses several points. First, he dismisses the claim that God cannot be described as having power over a truth truer than the Quran, noting that divine truth and the Quran are not subject to power in that sense. Then, he raises a question: Could God have caused every person He knows will disbelieve after puberty to die before puberty? If the opponent says no, they ascribe incapability to God; if yes, they affirm God's power to do the optimal since death saves such a person from eternal hellfire. The opponent might evade by claiming there is no optimal in such cases, but the author asserts he has already answered that. God is also able not to grant intellect to such people. Next, he asks about the disbeliever’s power to disbelieve: If God is able not to give that power, which is more optimal? If the opponent says not giving it is optimal, then giving it abandons the optimal. If the opponent says giving it is optimal despite knowledge of disbelief, that is obstinate. If they bring up the topic of ta'rid (indirect expression), it was already discussed. Finally, if they argue that without the power to disbelieve the person would not be morally responsible (mukallaf), the author replies that liberation from responsibility is a great benefit, saving one from eternal torment.

Key Terms
إماتة
أصلح
تعجيز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God cannot be said to have power over what is optimally beneficial for individuals.
Correction: The argument shows that God's power to cause death before puberty proves He has power over the optimal, since death saves from eternal punishment.

Checkpoint
What does Nasafi argue is the consequence if someone says God cannot cause a potential disbeliever to die before puberty?
A) They affirm God's power
B) They attribute incapability to God
C) They deny moral responsibility
D) They concede the point

Key Takeaway
If God can cause death before puberty to prevent disbelief, He has power over the optimal for that individual, saving them from hell.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God caused your death before you could commit a major sin, would you consider that a mercy or a loss? How does this passage challenge your understanding of what is truly 'good' for you?
?
Are there areas in your life where you doubt God's wisdom because you only see the worldly harm? What would it mean for you to trust that God's choice is always optimal, even when it seems harsh?
?
The passage argues that God could prevent disbelief by taking away intellect. How would you feel if God had not granted you the intellect to know Him? Does this thought increase your gratitude for your intellect and your responsibility to use it to seek truth?

The Power of Disbelief and Moral Responsibility
Nasafi discusses whether giving the power of disbelief is optimal and addresses moral responsibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that giving the power of disbelief is not optimal and that removal of moral responsibility is a benefit.

Core Idea
Giving the power to disbelieve, when God knows it will be used, is not optimal; freedom from moral responsibility is a benefit.

Nasafi continues by addressing another objection: whether it is optimal for God to give the disbeliever the power to disbelieve. He also responds to the concern that without that power, the person would not be morally responsible, arguing that this is actually a mercy.

Guided Path
1
Read the dialectical question
Nasafi asks whether it is more optimal to give or withhold the power of disbelief.
He establishes that God does give this power.
2
Analyse the first response
If the opponent says withholding is optimal, then giving it abandons the optimal.
3
Consider the second response
If the opponent says giving it is optimal despite knowledge of disbelief, that is obstinate.
Nasafi also dismisses the topic of ta'rid as already discussed.
4
Address the moral responsibility objection
If the opponent argues that without power the person is not mukallaf, Nasafi says that is a great benefit.
The person would not deserve eternal punishment.

Refutation of the Claim that Allah Cannot Have Power Over a More Truthful Statement
Nasafi refutes the claim that Allah cannot be described as having power over a statement more truthful than the Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's refutation of the claim that Allah cannot have power over a more truthful statement than the Qur'an, including the distinction between truth as an inherent attribute and as a contingent object of power.

Core Idea
Divine truth is not an object of power; it is an attribute.

This lesson examines a subtle argument from Nasafi against a Mu'tazilite claim. The opponents argued that Allah cannot be described as having power to produce a statement more truthful than the Qur'an. Nasafi responds by distinguishing between truth as an inherent attribute and as a contingent object of power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the literal translation. Notice how Nasafi presents the opponent's claim and his refutation.
The passage is dense; pay attention to the phrase 'built on their corrupt principle'.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
Identify what the Mu'tazilites are claiming: that Allah cannot be described as having power over a truth more truthful than the Qur'an. Note that Nasafi says this is based on a corrupt principle.
3
Understand Nasafi's distinction
Nasafi makes two points: (1) Allah's own truthfulness is not under power (it is an attribute), and (2) the Qur'an's truth is similarly not under power. Grasp why this distinction refutes the claim.
Think about the difference between an attribute of Allah and a created object.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider why this refutation matters: it preserves the uniqueness of the Qur'an and the intrinsic nature of divine attributes, preventing them from being treated as contingent acts of power.
This connects to broader topics in divine attributes and power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ما يزعمون أن الله تعالى لا يوصف بالقدرة على صدق أصدق من القرآن شيء بنوه على أصلهم الفاسد، فأما صدق الله تعالى فغير داخل تحت القدرة، وكذا القرآن على ما مر.
Then what they claim that Allah Most High cannot be described with power over a truthfulness more truthful than the Qur'an, they built it on their corrupt principle. As for the truthfulness of Allah Most High, it is not included under power, and likewise the Qur'an, as has passed.
Source pages p. 1015

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a Mu'tazilite claim. The author begins by stating the opponent's position: they claim that Allah cannot be described as having power (qudra) to produce a statement that is more truthful (asdaq) than the Qur'an. The author counters that this claim is built on a corrupt foundation (aslahum al-fasid). He then distinguishes: the truthfulness (sidq) of Allah Himself is not something that falls under divine power—because truth is an attribute of Allah, not something He creates or brings about. Similarly, the Qur'an, as the speech of Allah, is already the most truthful, and its truthfulness is not subject to being surpassed by an act of power. The argument implies that the opponent mistakenly treats truthfulness as a contingent object of power, whereas it is an inherent attribute.

Key Terms
القدرة
الصدق
القرآن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the author denies Allah's power over the Qur'an.
Correction: Nasafi affirms that the Qur'an's truth is intrinsic, not a product of power, so Allah is not described as having power over it in that sense.

Checkpoint

A) Because the Qur'an is the most truthful and its truth is beyond power
B) Because Allah's power is limited
C) Because the Mu'tazilites deny divine power
D) Because the Qur'an is created and thus can be surpassed

Key Takeaway
Nasafi demonstrates that divine truthfulness and the Qur'an's truth are inherent attributes, not contingent objects of divine power.

?

?
If the Qur'an's truth is not under power, then it cannot be improved or surpassed. Does this settle any unease you've had about the finality of the Qur'an? What practical difference will this make in how you approach its commands?

Argument That Power Must Be for Both Opposites and Allah is Free
Nasafi argues that power (qudra) is for both opposites and that Allah is free.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses the Mu'tazilite premise about power to prove that Allah acts by free choice and is not compelled.

Core Idea
Power is for both opposites; what lacks this is compulsion, not power.

In this passage, Nasafi engages in a dialectical proof with the Mu'tazilites. He first establishes a shared premise: power must be suitable for both opposites. Then he forces the opponent to admit that Allah's power, as affirmed in the Quran, covers actions that would cause universal disbelief. This shows Allah is free, not compelled.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the dialectical question-and-answer style.
The passage uses rhetorical questions to force the opponent's acknowledgment.
2
Identify the initial premise
Nasafi begins with the Mu'tazilite premise that power is suitable for both opposites. Also note the converse: what is not suitable is compulsion.
This premise is shared by both sides.
3
Follow the dialectical questions
Nasafi asks whether Allah is compelled or free, and whether He acts by power. Trace how the opponent must answer.
The answer is forced: Allah is powerful and acts by choice.
4
Examine the Quranic evidence
Read the two cited verses (42:27 and 43:33). They show Allah has power over acts that would lead to disbelief, proving His power covers both opposites.
These verses demonstrate that Allah's power is not limited to one outcome.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that Allah's power is for both opposites, so He is free (mukhtār), not compelled.
This refutes any notion that Allah's power is constrained.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : أليس أن من قولكم أن القدرة تصلح للضدين وأن كل ما لا يصلح لضد لا يصلح لضد ذلك الضد بل ما لا يصلح للضدين فهو اضطرار؟ فلا بد من بلى. قيل : أتقولون أن الله تعالى مضطر أم مختار ؟ وهل يفعل ما يفعل عن قدرة أم لا؟ فلابد من القول إنه تعالى قادر يفعل ما يفعل عن اختيار وقدرة. قيل : أليس أن الله تعالى أثبت لنفسه القدرة على ما لو فعل لكفر به عباده كلهم بقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَلَوْ بَسَطَ الله الرِّزْقَ لِعِبَادِهِ لَبَغَوْا فِي الْأَرْضِ ﴾ وقوله : ﴿ وَلَوْلَا أَن يَكُونَ النَّاسُ أُمَّةً وَاحِدَةً لَجَعَلْنَا لِمَن يَكْفُرُ بِالرَّحْمَنِ لِبُيُوتِهِمْ سُقْفًا مِن فِضَّةٍ وَمَعَارِجَ عَلَيْهَا يَظْهَرُونَ ﴾ فلا بد من بلى.
Then we say: Is it not from your statement that power is suitable for both opposites, and that whatever is not suitable for one opposite is not suitable for the opposite of that opposite? Rather, whatever is not suitable for both opposites is compulsion. There is no avoiding 'yes'. It is said: Do you say that Allah Most High is compelled or free? And does He do what He does by power or not? There is no avoiding saying that He is powerful, doing what He does by choice and power. It is said: Is it not that Allah Most High has affirmed for Himself power over that which if He did, all His servants would disbelieve, by His saying: 'And if Allah had extended provision for His servants, they would have transgressed in the earth' and His saying: 'And were it not that the people would become one community, We would have made for those who disbelieve in the Most Merciful, for their houses, ceilings of silver and staircases upon which they ascend'? There is no avoiding 'yes'.
Source pages p. 1016

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical proof (dalil) regarding the nature of divine power (qudra). The author begins by establishing the Mu'tazilite premise that power is suitable (sālih) for both opposites (al-ḍiddayn). That is, if a power can produce one act, it can also produce its opposite. What is not suitable for either opposite is not power but compulsion (iḍṭirār). The opponent must accept this. Then the author poses a question: Do you say that Allah is compelled (muḍṭarr) or free (mukhtār)? Does He act by power or not? The answer must be that He is powerful and acts by choice and power. Next, the author cites Quranic evidence that Allah has power over actions that, if done, would cause all people to disbelieve—such as giving excessive provision (Quran 42:27) or giving luxury to the disbelievers (Quran 43:33). These verses show that Allah has power to do things that would result in universal disbelief. The opponent must admit this. The argument thus forces the Mu'tazilite to recognize that Allah's power embraces both opposites (e.g., causing belief or disbelief), and that He is not compelled.

Key Terms
القدرة
الضدان
الاضطرار
المختار
الاختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Allah's power is restricted to only one opposite, like only causing belief.
Correction: Nasafi argues that genuine power must be suitable for both opposites. The Quran shows Allah has power over acts that would cause disbelief, proving His power is not limited.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is the consequence if a power is not suitable for both opposites?
A) It is still genuine power
B) It is compulsion (iḍṭirār)
C) It is non-existent
D) It is limited to one act

Key Takeaway
Power is inherently for both opposites; otherwise it is compulsion, and the Quran proves Allah has such power, so He is free.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah's power encompasses both opposites, including the ability to cause you to disbelieve, what does that imply about your own reliance on His guidance? How does this truth make you fear losing faith, and what are you doing daily to cling to His rope?
?
The Mu'tazilites argued that power must be for both opposites, yet they denied that Allah could be the creator of evil actions. Where in your own life do you subtly try to limit Allah's power by assuming He cannot or would not do certain things based on your own understanding?
?
Recognizing that Allah is free and not compelled forces you to confront your own compulsion to sin. If you are not acting by genuine choice but by habit or pressure, what does that reveal about your relationship with your Creator? How will you take responsibility today?

The Premise of Power for Opposites
Nasafi uses the Mu'tazila's definition of qudra to establish divine power over opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's own definition of qudra and how Nasafi uses it to prove that Allah is not compelled and has power over all opposites, including causing universal disbelief.

Core Idea
Power that cannot oppose is not power; Allah's power is absolute.

This lesson begins the refutation by reminding the Mu'tazila of their own principle: qudra (power) is only genuine if it can be used for two opposites. Nasafi then questions whether Allah is compelled or free, and proves from the Qur'an that Allah has power over causing universal disbelief. This establishes that Allah's power is unlimited and not bound by human notions of benefit.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Let the dialectical style sink in.
This passage has three clear moves.
2
Identify the shared premise
Notice that Nasafi starts with 'Is it not from your statement...' — he uses the Mu'tazila's own view that qudra is for opposites.
If power cannot produce opposites, it is compulsion (idtirar).
3
Follow the questioning
The questions force the opponent to admit that Allah is not compelled and that He has power over the opposite of causing universal disbelief, as shown by Quranic verses.
The two Quranic verses show that Allah withholds blessings to prevent disbelief and transgression.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: Why is it important to establish that Allah has power over causing disbelief? Consider how this prepares the ground for the al-aslah argument.
This proves that Allah's power extends to what He does not actually do.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : أليس أن من قولكم أن القدرة تصلح للضدين وأن كل ما لا يصلح لضد لا يصلح لضد ذلك الضد بل ما لا يصلح للضدين فهو اضطرار؟ فلا بد من بلى. قيل : أتقولون أن الله تعالى مضطر أم مختار ؟ وهل يفعل ما يفعل عن قدرة أم لا؟ فلابد من القول إنه تعالى قادر يفعل ما يفعل عن اختيار وقدرة. قيل : أليس أن الله تعالى أثبت لنفسه القدرة على ما لو فعل لكفر به عباده كلهم بقوله تعالى: ( وَلَوْ بَسَطَ الله الرِّزْقَ لِعِبَادِهِ لَبَغَوْا فِي الْأَرْضِ ) وقوله : ( وَلَوْلَا أَن يَكُونَ النَّاسُ أُمَّةً وَاحِدَةً لَجَعَلْنَا لِمَن يَكْفُرُ بِالرَّحْمَنِ لِبُيُوتِهِمْ سُقُفًا مِن فِضَّةٍ وَمَعَارِجَ عَلَيْهَا يَظْهَرُونَ ) فلا بد من بلى. قيل : إذا كان قادرًا على ما لو فعل لكفر به الخلق فهل تصفون بأنه قادر على ما لو فعل لآمن به الخلق؟ فإن قالوا: لا. فقد قصروا قدرته على أحد الضدين وهو عندهم اضطرار والقول به كفر وإن قالوا: نعم. قلنا : فإذا لم يفعل فقد ترك ما هو الأصلح لهم؟ فإن قالوا: لم يفعل ذلك لأنه لو فعل ثم آمنوا لكان ثواب إيمانهم أقل أو لم يكن لإيمانهم ثواب قلنا : ما قولكم ما القليل من الثواب على الإيمان أصلح لهم، أم الخلود في النار ؟ فإن قالوا : القليل من الثواب كان أصلح لهم. قد أقروا أنه ترك ما هو الأصلح لهم وإن قالوا : الخلود في النار أصلح. فقد تجانفوا وجعلوا نفوسهم ضحكة للخلق. ثم نقول لهم : إذا كانت قدرة الكفر وقدرة الإيمان واحدة، فقد أعطى الله تعالى كلا من الممتحنين تلك القدرة ، فلم قلتم أن الله تعالى فعل بعباده، ما هو الأصلح لهم وهو لم يعطهم إلا ما هو صالح للأمرين جميعا خصوصا في حق من علم أنه يكفر ولا يؤمن؟ وبم كنتم أولى ممن يقولون إنه فعل بعباده ما هو الأفسد لهم إذ أعطاهم ما يحصلون به الكفر خصوصا في حق من علم أنه يكفر ؟ وهذا مما لا اتصال لهم عنه، والله الموفق.
Then we say: Is it not from your statement that power is suitable for opposites, and that whatever is not suitable for an opposite is not suitable for the opposite of that opposite, rather whatever is not suitable for the two opposites is compulsion? So there must be an affirmative. It was said: Do you say that Allah is compelled or is He willing? And does He do what He does by power or not? So it must be said that He is capable, doing what He does by choice and power. It was said: Did not Allah prove for Himself the power over that which if He did, all His servants would disbelieve in Him by His words: 'And if Allah had extended provision for His servants, they would have transgressed in the earth' and His words: 'And were it not that mankind would be one community, We would have made for those who disbelieve in the Most Merciful, for their houses, ceilings of silver and stairways upon which to ascend'? So there must be an affirmative. It was said: If He is capable of that which if He did, the creation would disbelieve, do you describe Him as capable of that which if He did, the creation would believe? If they say: No. Then they have limited His power to one of the two opposites, and that according to them is compulsion, and saying it is disbelief. And if they say: Yes. We say: Then if He did not do it, He has left what is most beneficial for them? If they say: He did not do that because if He did and then they believed, the reward of their faith would be less or their faith would have no reward. We say: What do you say: Is a little reward for faith more beneficial for them, or eternity in the Fire? If they say: A little reward was more beneficial for them. They have admitted that He left what is most beneficial for them. And if they say: Eternity in the Fire is more beneficial. Then they have deviated and made themselves a laughingstock for the creation. Then we say to them: If the power for disbelief and the power for faith are one, then Allah has given each of those tested that power. So why do you say that Allah has done for His servants what is most beneficial for them, while He has not given them except that which is suitable for both matters, especially in the case of one whom He knows will disbelieve and not believe? And how are you more entitled than those who say that He has done for His servants what is most corrupt for them, since He gave them that by which they obtain disbelief, especially in the case of one whom He knows will disbelieve? And this is something from which they have no escape, and Allah is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 1016

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical refutation of the Mu'tazila view on divine power (qudra) and the doctrine of al-aslah (most beneficial). The author begins by establishing a premise the opponent accepts: power (qudra) is suitable for opposites (diddayn); what is not suitable for opposites is compulsion (idtirar). He then questions whether Allah is compelled or free, and shows that Allah's power over the opposite of creating universal disbelief (as shown in Quranic verses) implies He also has power over universal belief. The author then forces the opponent into a dilemma: if Allah is capable of causing universal belief but does not, then He has left what is most beneficial (al-aslah) for His servants. If the opponent claims that causing belief would reduce reward, the author asks which is better: a small reward or eternal punishment? Either admission leads to absurdity. Finally, he argues that since the same power is given to all tested, those who disbelieve are given the means to disbelieve, so Allah's action cannot be called 'most beneficial' without contradiction. The author concludes that the Mu'tazila have no escape from this refutation.

Key Terms
القدرة
الضدين
الاضطرار
الإيمان
الكفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Allah cannot have power over causing disbelief because it would contradict His goodness.
Correction: The passage shows that Allah indeed has power over causing disbelief, as proven by Quranic verses, but He chooses not to use it due to wisdom.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what does it mean if something is not suitable for two opposites?
A) It is perfect power.
B) It is compulsion (idtirar).
C) It is free will.
D) It is impossible.

Key Takeaway
Allah's power is absolute and extends to all opposites, including the ability to cause universal disbelief, but He acts by wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you ever stop to consider that Allah has the absolute power to make you disbelieve, but He withholds that power by His wisdom? How does that awareness change how you view your own failures and His mercy?
?
The Mu'tazila defined power as suitability for opposites. In practice, do you unconsciously limit God by assuming He must act only in ways you find good? Will you surrender your expectation of what God 'should' do and trust His wisdom even when He does not act?
?
If Allah's power extends to what He does not do, what does that imply for your prayers and your reliance on His action? Do you truly believe that He could answer in any way but may have a wise reason not to? Will you commit to trusting His choice over your own desires?

The Dilemma of al-Aslah
Nasafi presents a dilemma on al-aslah and concludes with the impossibility of the doctrine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi forces the Mu'tazila into a logical dilemma regarding al-aslah, exposing the incoherence of their doctrine.

Core Idea
If Allah could cause universal belief but doesn't, that contradicts al-aslah.

Building on the previous lesson, Nasafi now pushes the Mu'tazila to admit that Allah also has power over the opposite of universal belief. He then forces them into a dilemma: either Allah left what is most beneficial for His servants, or they must defend absurd consequences. The argument concludes by showing that the same power given to all tests refutes the idea that Allah always does what is best for each individual.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the argument
Read the Arabic passage for Lesson 2, then its translation. Notice the dialectical back-and-forth.
This passage has multiple objections and replies.
2
Trace the dilemma about al-aslah
Nasafi asks: if Allah is capable of making all believe but doesn't, then He has left what is best for them. The opponent tries to avoid this by claiming reward would be less or nonexistent.
Consider the two possible responses and their absurdities.
3
Analyze the final argument about shared power
Nasafi concludes that since the same power is given to all, and some use it for disbelief, Allah's giving that power cannot be called 'most beneficial' for those who disbelieve.
This refutes the Mu'tazila's claim that Allah always does al-aslah for each individual.
4
Reflect on the rhetorical strategies
Identify how Nasafi uses the opponent's own premises to trap them. The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.
The Mu'tazila are left with no satisfactory answer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : أليس أن من قولكم أن القدرة تصلح للضدين وأن كل ما لا يصلح لضد لا يصلح لضد ذلك الضد بل ما لا يصلح للضدين فهو اضطرار؟ فلا بد من بلى. قيل : أتقولون أن الله تعالى مضطر أم مختار ؟ وهل يفعل ما يفعل عن قدرة أم لا؟ فلابد من القول إنه تعالى قادر يفعل ما يفعل عن اختيار وقدرة. قيل : أليس أن الله تعالى أثبت لنفسه القدرة على ما لو فعل لكفر به عباده كلهم بقوله تعالى: ( وَلَوْ بَسَطَ الله الرِّزْقَ لِعِبَادِهِ لَبَغَوْا فِي الْأَرْضِ ) وقوله : ( وَلَوْلَا أَن يَكُونَ النَّاسُ أُمَّةً وَاحِدَةً لَجَعَلْنَا لِمَن يَكْفُرُ بِالرَّحْمَنِ لِبُيُوتِهِمْ سُقُفًا مِن فِضَّةٍ وَمَعَارِجَ عَلَيْهَا يَظْهَرُونَ ) فلا بد من بلى. قيل : إذا كان قادرًا على ما لو فعل لكفر به الخلق فهل تصفون بأنه قادر على ما لو فعل لآمن به الخلق؟ فإن قالوا: لا. فقد قصروا قدرته على أحد الضدين وهو عندهم اضطرار والقول به كفر وإن قالوا: نعم. قلنا : فإذا لم يفعل فقد ترك ما هو الأصلح لهم؟ فإن قالوا: لم يفعل ذلك لأنه لو فعل ثم آمنوا لكان ثواب إيمانهم أقل أو لم يكن لإيمانهم ثواب قلنا : ما قولكم ما القليل من الثواب على الإيمان أصلح لهم، أم الخلود في النار ؟ فإن قالوا : القليل من الثواب كان أصلح لهم. قد أقروا أنه ترك ما هو الأصلح لهم وإن قالوا : الخلود في النار أصلح. فقد تجانفوا وجعلوا نفوسهم ضحكة للخلق. ثم نقول لهم : إذا كانت قدرة الكفر وقدرة الإيمان واحدة، فقد أعطى الله تعالى كلا من الممتحنين تلك القدرة ، فلم قلتم أن الله تعالى فعل بعباده، ما هو الأصلح لهم وهو لم يعطهم إلا ما هو صالح للأمرين جميعا خصوصا في حق من علم أنه يكفر ولا يؤمن؟ وبم كنتم أولى ممن يقولون إنه فعل بعباده ما هو الأفسد لهم إذ أعطاهم ما يحصلون به الكفر خصوصا في حق من علم أنه يكفر ؟ وهذا مما لا اتصال لهم عنه، والله الموفق.

The Wise Being: Needy or Independent?
Disjunctive question on which wise being must give all.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the obligation to give all one's power depends on whether the wise being is needy or independent, and why the needy analogy cannot apply to God.

Core Idea
God is independent; His obligations follow His nature.

The author begins by challenging the assumption that a wise being must expend all his power to secure obedience. He poses a crucial disjunction: is this obligation upon a needy wise being who depends on others, or upon an independent wise being who needs nothing? The answer determines the nature of the obligation.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening question
Read the Arabic: 'على أي حكيم يجب ما ذكرت من بذل جميع ما في مقدوره...' and its translation. Notice how the question sets up the entire argument.
2
Identify the two options
The author presents two types of wise beings: one in need of obedience, and one independent. Distinguish them clearly.
This is a classic disjunction (تقسيم) used in kalam.
3
Understand the responses
If they say the needy one, it's conceded but cannot apply to God. If they say the independent one, it's rejected. That means the obligation is not on God.
The author is leading to the conclusion that God is not obligated to give all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال: على أي حكيم يجب ما ذكرت من بذل جميع ما في مقدوره لمن دعاه إلى طاعته وطلب رجوعه عن معصيته؟ أعلى حكيم محتاج إلى طاعة المطيع، ورجوع العدو عن العداوة متعذر بكثرة أعوانه وأنصاره وتكاتف حزبه وأوليائه ذليل عند كثرة أعدائه؟ أم على حكيم مستغن عن طاعة غيره إياه عزيز في ذاته قوي في سلطانه لا يضعفه كثرة الأعداء ولا يقويه كثرة الأولياء؟ فإن قالوا: بالأول. فهو مسلم، ولكن لا وجه إلى تعديته إلى الغائب. وإن قالوا: بالثاني. فهو ممنوع ثم نقول لهم: لو كان الأمر على ما ذكرتم أن الأمر كان مريدًا لطاعة المأمور محبًا لها، وكذا لرجوع العدو لكان ما قلت مسلمًا ولكان يعطي لا محالة ويبذل ما يحتاج إليه المأمور للامتثال ولكن لم قلتم أن البار جل وعلا مريد لطاعة الكافر وأن أمره بها، ومريد لرجوع العدو وإن دعاه إلى الرجوع ونهاه عن العداوة والعصيان؟ ولم قلتم أن الحكيم إذا كان أمره لمن علم أنه لا يأتمر لإلزام الحجة لا لتحصيل المأمور به أنه وجب عليه بذل ما ذكرت؟ ثم نقول: إن أمر الله تعالى للكافر بالإيمان ونهيه إياه عن الكفر مع علمه أنه يكفر ولا يؤمن ما كان ليفعل إذ في فعله تجهيله وتكذيبه فيما أخبر بقوله: لَأَمْلَأَنَّ جَهَنَّمَ (الآية)، ومن أراد ذلك وأعان عليه وبذل لتحصيل ذلك أقصى ما في مقدوره لكان من أسفه السفهاء حيث بذل مقدوره في إزالة وإبطال سلطانه ومقدرته، بل كان لإلزام الحجة واستيجابهم التعذيب في النيران بعصيانهم ليصير ما يتحقق به علمه ويتأكد خبره من تعذيبهم خالدًا مخلدًا عدلاً منه وحكمة، وإذا كان الأمر على ذلك لم يكن بذله أقصى ما في مقدوره ونهاية ما في قوته واجبًا بل إعطاء قدر ما يصح به التكليف كاف، وقد مر تقرير هذا في مسألة الإرادة.
Then it is said: Upon which wise being is what you mentioned obligatory, namely expending all that is in his power for one who calls him to his obedience and seeks his return from disobedience? Upon a wise being who is in need of the obedience of the obedient, and the return of the enemy from enmity is hindered by the multitude of his supporters and helpers and the solidarity of his party and allies, and he is abased by the multitude of his enemies? Or upon a wise being who is independent of the obedience of others to him, mighty in His essence, strong in His authority, whose might is not weakened by the multitude of enemies nor strengthened by the multitude of allies? If they say: the first. Then it is conceded, but there is no way to transfer it to the unseen. And if they say: the second. Then it is rejected. Then we say to them: If the matter were as you mentioned, that the commander desires the obedience of the commanded and loves it, and similarly the return of the enemy, then what you said would be conceded, and He would indeed give and expend what the commanded needs for compliance. But why do you say that the Lord, glorified and exalted, desires the faith of the disbeliever and commands him with it, and desires the return of the enemy, even though He calls him to return and forbids him from enmity and disobedience? And why do you say that the wise being, when He commands someone He knows will not comply, in order to establish the proof (ilzam al-hujja) and not to achieve the commanded thing, that it is obligatory upon Him to expend what you mentioned? Then we say: Indeed, the command of God most high to the disbeliever to have faith and His prohibition of him from unbelief, along with His knowledge that he will disbelieve and not believe, was not done to make him do it, because in doing that would be making Him foolish and belying His statement when He said: 'I will surely fill Hell' (the verse). And whoever desires that and helps towards it and expends for achieving that the utmost of what is in his power would be among the most foolish of fools, because he expended his power in removing and invalidating his own authority and power. Rather, it was for establishing the proof and making them deserve punishment in the fires due to their disobedience, so that what His knowledge realizes and His news affirms of their punishment becomes everlasting and eternal, out of justice from Him and wisdom. And when the matter is so, then expending the utmost of what is in His power and the limit of His strength is not obligatory; rather, giving enough to make the command valid is sufficient. And the clarification of this has passed in the issue of the will (mas'alat al-irada).
Source pages p. 1018

Commentary
This passage presents a disjunctive argument about the obligation of a wise being to expend all his power to secure obedience. The author asks: upon which type of wise being is it obligatory? Either a needy one who depends on others' obedience, or an independent one. If they say the first, it is conceded but cannot be applied to God (the unseen). If they say the second, it is rejected. Then the author questions the assumption that God desires the disbeliever's faith and obedience. He argues that God's command to the disbeliever is not intended to actually make him believe, because if God truly desired it, He would have to give everything to secure it, which would be foolish (since it would undermine His own decree). Rather, the command is for establishing the proof (ilzam al-hujja) against them, so that their punishment is just and eternal. Therefore, God is not obligated to give the utmost; giving enough to make the command valid (i.e., the ability to comply) suffices. The author refers back to the discussion on divine will.

Key Terms
الحكيم
غائب
شاهد
مقدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God, like a human ruler, must do everything possible to secure obedience because He commands it.
Correction: God is not needy; His command is for establishing proof, not because He needs obedience.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a wise being is independent and self-sufficient, what is the ruling on the obligation to give all power?
A) It is obligatory
B) It is permissible but not obligatory
C) It is rejected (ممنوع)
D) It depends on the situation

Key Takeaway
The obligation to give all power depends on the nature of the wise being; God, being independent, has no such obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever act as if God needs your worship to be complete, forgetting that He is independent of all creatures? How would realizing His independence change your relationship with Him?
?
In your life, when you obey God, do you secretly believe you are doing Him a favor? What does this passage teach you about your obedience adding nothing to His Majesty?
?
The passage contrasts a needy wise being and an independent one. Which of these two descriptions matches the God you truly believe in? Not just in theory, but in the way you live your daily struggles?
